#### [Get package information with dpkg and APT](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-package-information-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-package-information-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980&t=0)** - Debian-based distributions have their own package file format and [Package Management](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Package%20Management.md) system. These commands can be used on any Debian-based systems, such as Debian, [Ubuntu](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Ubuntu.md), and [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) Mint. In this video, we will talk about how to get information about which packages are available to install, as well as information about the packages themselves. The low-level command for managing packages is dpkg, or Debian package. This is the equivalent to the rpm command on Red Hat-based systems. The repository-based package management system is APT, or Advanced Packaging Tool, and would be equivalent to yum or dnf on Red Hat. You will see references to apt-get and apt-cache tools on the Internet. These tools are very powerful and still work to query as well as install packages. However, there is a new tool, simply called apt, that provides a more user-friendly experience, so we'll focus on it in this course, and only reference the other commands when necessary. We have several commands that will search for packages in a repository. Apt search shows all packages, and shows if they're installed or not. Apt show shows package details. Apt list lists packages based on criteria such as installed or upgradable. Let's use these commands to find some packages, specifically, the whois tool. Open a terminal and type in sudo apt search whois and hit Enter. Type in your password if prompted. This searches the package names as well as the description and files included in the package. If I scroll up to the top of the list, I can see that the first package is installed. Now, let's get more information on whois.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-package-information-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980&t=94)** Type in clear, and then type in sudo apt info whois and hit Enter. From this information, we can see the install size, the version, the description, its dependencies, and who to contact if we want to file a bug. A lot of people use apt-cache to get a list of installed packages, as it gives you a nice, clean list. The apt command also has this functionality. If you want a list of installed packages named whois, type in clear and then type in sudo apt list --installed whois and hit Enter. The output isn't as clear as it could be. It says it is listing and then done without showing anything. This means it's not installed. Let's remove the --installed option and hit Enter again. This shows us what is available. Notice that it shows both 32-bit and 64-bit architectures. Let's install whois and look again. Type in clear. Type in sudo apt install whois and hit Enter. Notice how we have a progress bar, and that the apt command did not ask for confirmation. It didn't ask for confirmation because the operation will only install what we requested, and nothing more. If this install pulled in some other packages, such as dependencies, then it would have prompted us. Now, let's list again. Type in clear and then type in sudo apt list whois and hit Enter. Now we can see that it's installed. There's another difference between the Debian package management system and Red Hat's yum. With yum, we have package groups. If we install a group, we install all packages in the group. Debian does not have a group concept,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-package-information-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980&t=188)** but uses virtual packages, which do not have anything in them. These virtual packages require other packages. If we install a virtual package, it will install all of its dependencies, too. The functionality is the same, but the method is different. Let's take a look at one. Type in clear and then type in sudo apt info apache and hit Enter. The output from this says apache is a virtual package and we can't get information on it. The low-level dpkg command gives us the ability to query local software packages. First, let's download a package. To do so, we'll use the apt-get download command, which will only download the package and not install it. Let's move to the downloads directory first. Type in cd ~/Downloads with a capital D and hit Enter. Now, type in apt-get download whois and hit Enter. Now, list the current directory with ls. We can see that we have a Debian package downloaded. Now, let's use the dpkg command to get information on this package. Type in clear and then type in sudo dpkg --info whois and then press your Tab key and hit Enter. You can see that the information provided by this command is the same as the apt info output. Keep in mind this works on local packages only. Now, let's see which files will be installed if we install it. Type in clear and then type in sudo dpkg -c whois and hit your Tab key again. This is handy information to know before we install software.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-package-information-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980&t=281)** Now, let's install it and query the package database. Type in clear and then type in sudo dpkg -i, for install, space, whois and hit your Tab key. Now, let's ask the package database to list the files. Type in sudo dpkg -L, for list, space, whois and hit Enter. Notice that we're not using the software package name, but rather, the name of the application. For installed applications, we use the application name, not the package name. The last thing we'll do is ask the package database which package a file in the file system belongs to. For this, we'll use the /usr/bin/whois file. Type in clear and then type in sudo dpkg -S /usr/bin/whois and hit Enter.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-package-information-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980&t=331)** We can see that it belongs to the whois package that we just installed. We can do this with any file in the file system that was installed by dpkg or APT.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Package Management](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Package%20Management.md) (3), [Ubuntu](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Ubuntu.md) (1), [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) (1)
> **CLI Commands:** apt (20), sudo (11), yum (3), apache (2), dnf (1)

> **Tools:** terminal (1)

#### [Manage software with dpkg and APT](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-software-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-software-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We have several Debian tools for installing and removing software. In the past, we would have used the apt-get install command to install software from a repository. In this course, we'll be focusing on the new APT tool. Let's install the Apache Web server. In a terminal type in sudo apt install apache2 and hit enter. Type in your password if prompted. This time it prompts us because it's going to install software that we didn't specify, namely dependencies of the Apache2 to package. Say yes. To verify let's do an APT info. Type in clear and then type in sudo apt info apache2 and hit enter. Here we see that it's installed, what it provides and a list of its dependencies. It also gives us a list of other suggested packages, which is nice. Since we just installed this package, there won't be any upgrades to it. However, we can get a list of software to upgrade by using the APT list command again. Type in clear and then type in sudo apt list --upgradable and hit enter.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-software-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980&t=65)** This list is software packages that have newer versions available. If there aren't any packages in this list, we may want to update the indexes of available software. To do so type in clear then type in sudo apt update and hit enter. This will update the indexes of available software. When that's done, bring your APT list line back and hit enter. Now let's pick a package and upgrade it. I'm going to select vim-common from this list. You can choose a different package. Type in clear. Now to upgrade it type in sudo apt upgrade vim-common or the package that you chose. APT will tell us what it's going to do and prompts for confirmation. Press y to confirm. To remove a package we can use APT or dpkg. Type in clear and then let's remove the Apache2 package with APT. Type in sudo apt remove apache2 and hit enter. Notice that we installed Apache2, a lot of dependencies installed with it. But when removing the package, it doesn't automatically remove the dependencies. It does however, tell us how to get rid of them which we'll do in a moment. Press y to continue. To remove all packages that are no longer needed, type in clear and then type in sudo apt autoremove and hit enter and press Y to continue. We can also use dpkg to remove packages. Keep in mind that dpkg doesn't manage dependencies. Type in clear.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-software-with-dpkg-and-apt?u=76281980&t=158)** Type in sudo dpkg -r whois and hit enter. This works fine for simpler jobs, but for uninstalling complex dependency trees I'd use APT. One more thing to note, that when you uninstall a package it leaves behind the configuration files. If you want to remove the package and its configuration files use APT purge. Type in clear and then type in sudo apt purge apache2 and hit enter and say Yes to continue. This will get rid of the configuration files that were left behind. If you want to upgrade the entire distribution to a newer version as opposed to just updating packages within the distribution version, use APT full-upgrade. Type in clear then type in sudo apt full-upgrade and hit enter. I don't have a distribution upgrade as I used the most recent version of [Ubuntu](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Ubuntu.md). Depending on when you take this course, you may have a distribution to upgrade to.

#### [Work with APT repositories](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/work-with-apt-repositories?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/work-with-apt-repositories?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's generally not advisable to install software that we download for Web sites on the Internet. When you do so, you often lose the ability to check the software's digital signature which ensures the package hasn't been tampered with. In most Debian software repositories, the index of software is signed and not the packages themselves. Thus if a package is downloaded from the repository, tampered with and then stored on a Web site, a hacker could lure an unsuspecting user into downloading it. As such it's always better to get the software from software repositories. You can add to the pool of available software by adding new repositories to your APT configuration. From the desktop we can start the [Ubuntu](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Ubuntu.md) Software and Updates GUI by clicking on the Show Applications icon in the bottom of the dock and then typing in software and then click on Ubuntu Software and Updates. There are four section names for each repository. Main is the software supported by Ubuntu. Universe is open-source commutative software. Restricted are closed-source drivers. And lastly multiverse has proprietary or non-open source software. These names may vary on different Debian-based distributions. For each of these sections there are channels as well, named release, which is the standard channel; release-updates, which are the updates; release-security, which are only security updates; and lastly, release-backports, which are packages that have been backported to the current OS version. In addition, Ubuntu and other Debian-based distribution will have OS-version-specific repositories
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/work-with-apt-repositories?u=76281980&t=94)** and mirrors for them. For Ubuntu there's 17.10 ArtfulAardvark, 18.04 Bionic Beaver, 18.10 Cosmic Cuttlefish, and 19.04 Disco Dingo. The first section of the number is the year, such as 2019, and the last section is the month, such as the fourth month or April. For this course, I'm using Ubuntu 19.04 Disco Dingo. This will explain why you see the world Disco in many of these repository lines. Debian has a fairly complex versioning system. For instance, we may have a package for Ubuntu 19.04 that belongs to release updates since it's an update file that has a restrictive license, so it also belongs to the Multiverse channel. Lastly it may be compiled for 32-bit CPUs. In addition, we may choose a different mirror that will be faster for us. All of the information for each repository channel is stored in one line of the configuration file. You can enable some repositories that contain proprietary and closed-source packages by clicking on the Other Software tab and then clicking on Canonical Partners. We can also add additional repositories by clicking on the Add button and pasting in the APT line into it. We'll see what this looks like in a moment. Now close the GUI. The repositories are stored in /etc/apt/sources.list as well as files in the /etc/apt/sources.list.d directory. We can look at these easily using APT.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/work-with-apt-repositories?u=76281980&t=193)** Open a terminal and type in apt policy and hit enter. You can also look at the files directly using grep. Type in clear and then type in grep '^deb' /etc/apt/sources.list
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/work-with-apt-repositories?u=76281980&t=215)** /etc/apt/sources.list.d/* and hit enter. This shows only lines that start with the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "deb" in both the sources.list file and the sources.list.d directory. We can add repositories from the command line by editing the sources.list file manually and pasting in the APT line, or we can download a repo file into the sources.list.d directory. Deleting the repositories can also be done just by commenting out the line or deleting it by using a text editor. APT also includes a couple of commands to help with this process. First let's talk about adding generic repositories. For this exercise we'll add the repository for the Canonical Partner archive. Type in clear and then type in sudo add-apt-repository 'deb [http://archive.canonical.com/ubuntu](http://archive.canonical.com/ubuntu)/ disco partner'
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/work-with-apt-repositories?u=76281980&t=280)** and hit enter. Type in your password if prompted. This will force and APT update of the repository data. After it's done, the software from this repository will be available. To remove the repository, bring your line back and add --remove to the line. Type in clear then bring your line back add --remove and hit enter. Another way to add a third-party repository is through a PPA or Personal Package Archive. A PPA is usually a very focused repository run possibly by the authors of the software although it doesn't have to be. Let's add the PPA for LibreOffice. Type in clear and type in sudo add-apt-repository ppa:libreoffice/ppa and hit enter. Note that if you install software from this repository and then deleted the repository, there won't be any updates to that software. There's a note in the output that says if you want to remove all packages from the repository install ppa-purge. Now hit enter. This will spawn an APT update operation. Once that's done, you can install software from this repository. You can also remove the repository the same way as you added it just by adding --remove like we did earlier.

### 5. Use Red Hat Package Management

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [RPM software management overview](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/rpm-software-management-overview?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/rpm-software-management-overview?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've talked about the RPM command and the RPM package format already in this course. RPM knows what its dependencies are, and has the ability to download software from ftp and [Web Servers](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Web%20Servers.md). The thing that's missing is a repository-centric view. RPM does not manage software collections in remote repositories. As such, Yum was created by Yellowdog [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) and has since been adopted by many Red Hat-based distributions, including Fedora and CentOS. Yum resolves dependencies automatically. This means it downloads and installs all software packages necessary. This includes packages that the user didn't specify if the chosen package requires them. Yum has the concept of software package groups. A group is a list of software that is usually installed together. For instance, we could have a web server group that included the web server, and other tools commonly installed with it. Yum repositories contain RPM software packages. The Yum client maintains a local list of software repositories. Users can add repositories by just adding a new Yum configuration file for them. The Yum client also maintains a local list of all available software. The package install process using Yum was pretty straightforward. First, the Yum client contacts the repositories that it's aware of, and gets their list of software packages. These lists are then cached locally, and it updates them during install operations. The user then selects the software package that they want to install. Users can select packages to install by using either [CLI](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/CLI.md) or Gooey tools. Yum then calculates dependencies. This would be the requested software package,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/rpm-software-management-overview?u=76281980&t=94)** and any packages that it requires. Yum then downloads all software packages and installs them using the RPM libraries, just like the RPM command does. Once the install has finished, Yum updates the RPM package database. Installing software using Yum couldn't be easier. Yum also has the concept of software groups. Yum groups contain multiple software packages. These software packages are usually installed together. All software in a group can be installed at once. All software in a group can be removed at once. Groups can contain optional software. Optional software is related software but not installed by default. Groups make configuring a system quicker. Installing a group of software with one command is much easier than installing each package individually.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Web Servers](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Web%20Servers.md) (1), [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) (1), [CLI](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/CLI.md) (1)
> **CLI Commands:** yum (15), make (1)
> **Env Vars:** rpm (8), cli (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (5)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Analogies:** for instance (1), just like (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Query packages with RPM](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-packages-with-rpm?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-packages-with-rpm?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] RPM is used to install local software packages. Once a package is installed, the RPM package database is updated with the package information. We can query that package database. We can also query a package directly even if it isn't installed. In addition, we can query a file. In reality it's looking through the package database for a reference to the file. This only works for files that belong to software packages. Let's use the RPM command to query the database. In a terminal, type in: rpm -qa and hit enter. The -q option tells RPM to query and -a means all packages. We could specify the two options individually, but we can also place them next to each other as you see here. Because we haven't specified a package or a file to query, RPM will query the database. We can see from this list that there's a lot of packages installed. If you'd like to have a sorted list, just type it into sort. Type in rpm -qa | sort and hit enter and now you have an alphabetical list. Since we know everyone has bash installed, let's query that. Type in clear, and hit enter. Now type in rpm -qi, for query information, bash, and hit enter. Again, we haven't specified to query a package or a file, so this information is coming from the database. The -qi will show information about the package. In this information we see name, version, release, install date, and more. Near the bottom we see the description.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-packages-with-rpm?u=76281980&t=93)** We can also narrow our search based on single attributes. Let's say we wanted to find all packages in the system environment slash shells group. We can do this, we just need to specify it. Type in clear again, and then type in rpm -qa Group, with a capital G, equals, double quote, System with a capital S, space, Environment with a capital E, slash Shells, with a capital S, double quote, and hit enter. This shows the packages that have been tagged with system environment shells. We can also show when all packages were installed sorted by date. Type in rpm -qa --last and hit enter. If we want to see where all the files in a package were installed, we use -ql for query list. Type in clear, and then type in rpm -ql yum, and hit enter. This will query for the list of file paths. The yum command installs 180 different files into different directories. It's nice to have a database maintain these files. If we want to slim this list down to just documentation, we can specify the -d option. Type in clear, and then type in rpm -qd, for query documentation, space yum and hit enter. Or we could use the -c option to show only configuration files. Type in rpm -qc yum for query configuration files. If we find a file on disc and
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-packages-with-rpm?u=76281980&t=185)** want to know what package it came from, we can query the database for this as well. We'll use the -qf options. Type in rpm -qf, for query file, /bin/bash that would be the path of the file, and hit enter. This shows that the slash bin slash bash file came from the bash package. Being able to identify where software came from can help a lot in learning about our [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) system. What is really handy is combining the -qd for query documentation and qf for query file options. Type in clear, and then type in rpm -qdf /bin/bash, and this shows all of the documentation for the /bin/bash command. Any time you find a file on disk and want to know where the documentation is, use these options. We can also ask the database what this package provides using --provides. Type in clear, and then type in rpm -q, for query, --provides bash, and hit enter. These are the features that the bash package provides. If you want to see what it requires, you can type in rpm -q --requires bash, and hit enter. Another interesting query option is --changelog. This lets us check the changes to the package. Type in clear, and then type in rpm -q --changelog bash, and hit enter. This'll show all of the changes that
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-packages-with-rpm?u=76281980&t=279)** have happened to this package. So far we've only been querying the database. If we have an rpm file downloaded, we can query the package directly before it's installed. Let's make a directory to put our rpm's in. Type in mkdir /tmp/packages and hit enter. Make sure that you do not use sudo in this case. Now let's download an rpm. We're going to use the yum tool to do this to save time. We need to install the yum download only plugin first and then we'll download the httpd package. Type in sudo yum install -y yum-plugin-downloadonly and hit enter, and type in your password then hit enter again. As soon as that finishes, type in sudo yum install --downloadonly
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-packages-with-rpm?u=76281980&t=333)** - -downloaddir=/tmp/packages httpd.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-packages-with-rpm?u=76281980&t=342)** What this is going to do is download the httpd packages into the /tmp/packages directory. Now let's cd and do /tmp/packages and then type in ls. Yum would normally install the rpm from the repository after downloading it, however since we passed the --downloadonly option, and specified the /tmp/packages directory, with the --downloaddir option, it only downloaded the packages. Now that we typed in ls we can see that we have an httpd package, an httpd-tools package, and a mailcap package, all of which were downloaded with yum. To query a package that hasn't been installed yet, we'll add the -p option to rpm. Type in clear, and then type in ls. Type in rpm -qip -httpd-2, and then hit your tab key so it fills out the line and then hit enter. When we query the database we just provide a -qi. To query a package we provide -qip. To get a list of all files inside the package and where they'll be installed, provide -l. Type in rpm -qlp httpd-2, and then hit your tab key again to complete the line, and then hit enter. Many of the previous query options will also work on a package as well. Just make sure that you provide the -p option so it queries the package and not the database. For more information, check the man page for rpm, by typing in man rpm and hit enter.

If we're attempting to install 100 packages, and one has a dependency problem, the entire update will fail. If we don't want to hold up the other
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/intall-and-remove-packages-with-yum?u=76281980&t=201)** 99 packages from updating, while the repository maintainers are ironing out the problem, we can pass --skip-broken, to YUM, to have it install all packages that are not broken. When YUM removes a package, it leaves the configuration file behind, in case you're upgrading, or want to run that service in the future. > **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/intall-and-remove-packages-with-yum?u=76281980&t=320)** Notice that Wireshark has two dependencies. If we want to remove a package and its dependencies, if they aren't being used by other packages, then we can use the autoremove sub-command, so type in sudo yum autoremove wireshark, and hit enter.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/intall-and-remove-packages-with-yum?u=76281980&t=363)** Now we see that it's removing Wireshark, and its dependencies. Although YUM will take a -white argument for remove, and autoremove, I don't recommend it. If you wanted to remove one package, and all other software requires that package, YUM will attempt to uninstall your entire OS. Passing the -white option to YUM, means it won't ask you first, which could be disastrous. If you want to go one step further to clean up old, unused packages, we can use the packages-cleanup command from the yum-utils package. Let's make sure it's installed first. Type in clear, and then type in, sudo yum install -y yum-utils, and hit enter.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/intall-and-remove-packages-with-yum?u=76281980&t=404)** Now that package utils is installed, let's run package-cleanup, with the --leaves option, hit enter. Notice that package cleanup didn't do anything, but give us a list of packages that are no longer needed. It's still up to us to use YUM to remove them manually.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Install and remove package groups](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-remove-package-groups?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-remove-package-groups?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Managing package groups is not that different from managing packages. Every yum group has a group ID in addition to a group name. To get a list of all group names, including IDs, type in yum group list ids and hit Enter. This will show all groups, and inside parentheses, their group ID. The group ID is a more command line friendly name without spaces in it. You can manage groups using their group name, or their group ID. We can use the group name enclosed in double quotes, in this case, yum group install "Security Tools". We could also use the group ID, the group ID of the security tools group would be security-tools. So you type in yum group install security-tools. We can also manage groups using package tools. If we prefix the group names and IDs with the @ symbol. So we could type in yum install @"Security Tools". Or yum install @security-tools using the group ID. ### 6. Linux as a Virtualized Guest

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Compare cloud and virtualization concepts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/compare-cloud-and-virtualization-concepts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/compare-cloud-and-virtualization-concepts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We hear the terms [Cloud Computing](../../Topics/Cloud%20Computing.md) and virtualization a lot these days. In some cases, it seems that the terms are used interchangeably. Let's talk about these two terms and differentiate between them. First, let's talk about cloud computing. Cloud computing is the automatic and dynamic scaling of computer resources, especially [Data Storage](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Storage.md) and computing power. That last part is important. Expansion and contraction of available resources is done automatically based on need. Let's say we start out with only a slight demand for CPU power. In that case, our cloud would have one host running. As the demand increased, the cloud management software would spawn more hosts to handle the load. This process would continue to happen until all resources would be active and processing requests. As soon as the demand lessened, hosts would be shut down until the cloud would be running with the least number of hosts again. This allows us to save a great deal of resources including electricity. The reason we call it a cloud is that its size increases and decreases dynamically just like a cloud in the sky. Clouds expand depending on the resources they contain. In the case of a real cloud, the resource would be moisture for rain. Resources provided by cloud computing may include CPU cycles. This is a common use of cloud computing. [Amazon Web Services](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Amazon%20Web%20Services%20(AWS).md), [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Compute Cloud, and [Microsoft Azure](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Azure.md) provide these services. Providing dynamic storage is also popular in the cloud. Popular choices are Dropbox, [OneDrive](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20OneDrive.md), [Google Drive](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Google%20Drive.md), and pCloud. If 100 people subscribe to one terabyte of data storage each, a cloud provider won't immediately add 100 terabytes of storage. They'll wait until they're almost running out and then add it.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/compare-cloud-and-virtualization-concepts?u=76281980&t=92)** This is called thin provisioning. The hope is that even though one person may use one terabyte, the other 99 won't. Cloud database services are getting more common. By using a cloud database, you don't necessarily need to concern yourself with managing a database. You just use it. Examples of cloud database providers would be Amazon Relational Database Service, [Google Cloud](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Google%20Cloud%20Platform%20(GCP).md) [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md), and Microsoft Azure database services. Because we are effectively using CPUs, disks, and applications, we need networking in the cloud too. For this, we pay for network services. This would entail [Routing](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Routing.md), switching firewalls, VPNs, and [IP Addressing](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/IP%20Addressing.md). Notice that all of these things exist in the normal physical world as pieces of infrastructure. In the cloud, we're not directly purchasing the infrastructure but are, in fact, paying for the [infrastructure as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/IaaS.md), or [IaaS](../../Glossary/Concept/IaaS.md). In the past, we would have needed to build a large network full of expensive servers, disks, and network equipment. Now we can pay for it as a service. In infrastructure as a service, we might have CPU resources such as [Virtual Machines](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Virtual%20Machines.md) that we can log in to remotely and install the OS and software that we want. In this scenario, we're working at a fairly low level, just as we would if we built physical servers and networks. With cloud computing, we have the concept of everything as a service. The next level of an abstraction is [platform as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/PaaS.md), or [PaaS](../../Glossary/Concept/PaaS.md). At this level, we don't interact directly with the operating system but rather create applications on a platform. For instance, we could create an app on Google App Engine without any knowledge of which operating system it's running on.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/compare-cloud-and-virtualization-concepts?u=76281980&t=185)** With PaaS, we write to the API of the platform, not to the operating system. The next higher level in the abstraction is [software as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/SaaS.md). We've all used web-based applications such as Gmail. These are applications running somewhere on the internet. Somewhere, there are real servers, disks, and networks with operating systems running on them, but there's no way of telling that from the application itself. The software itself is the service we're paying for. Any of these services could either be in a private cloud hosted by us or a provider. It could be in a public cloud or a combination of both in a [Hybrid Cloud](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Hybrid%20Cloud.md). Some online services like Netflix have, in the past, provided their own video streaming servers, but if demand outstripped the resource pool, they would direct the request to [Cloud Services](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Cloud%20Services.md). This gives a company the ability to grow without risk. Notice that I haven't focused on virtualization yet. So what is virtualization? At a low level, it's replacing real physical resources with virtual ones in software. Virtualization is creating a virtual version of something, specifically computer [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md), storage devices, and network resources. If you want to virtualize a computer, we have a hypervisor that creates a software layer to intercept software requests for hardware. For example, in a Type 2 hypervisor like VirtualBox, our virtualized application would request a resource such as disk space. It would send that request to the virtualized guest operating system, which would then make the request to the virtual machine provided by the hypervisor. The hypervisor would then make the request to the host operating system, which then allocates the physical resource. In this course, we've been using VirtualBox
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/compare-cloud-and-virtualization-concepts?u=76281980&t=277)** to virtualize various [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) distributions. VirtualBox provides virtual CPUs, virtual hard drive controllers and virtual drives, network cards, virtual ports, display cards, and even a BIOS. The client OS doesn't even know it's running inside a virtual machine. Although virtualization probably isn't technically required for cloud computing, it would be hard to imagine not using it. It's fairly easy for cloud management software to start up more VMs when the demand requires it. As you can probably tell, virtualization is an important element in cloud computing, although you can use it by itself as well.

#### [Types of hypervisors](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/types-of-hypervisors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/types-of-hypervisors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the previous video, I outlined how a type two hypervisor work. The application requests resources such as memory disc or network from the Guest OS in the Virtual Machine. The VM then requests resources from the hypervisor, which in turn asks the Host OS, which then accesses the resources from the physical machine. There are different types of hypervisors, but generally they're categorized as type one or type two. In the case of a type two hypervisor, such as VirtualBox, It's an application running on a Host OS, which in turn provides [Virtual Machines](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Virtual%20Machines.md) for Guest Operating Systems. In a type one hypervisor, the application requests the resources from the Guest OS and the VM, which in turn requests the resources from the hypervisor. So far it's the same. Where they differ though is that the hypervisors running on what we call Bare Metal, this means that there's no Host OS to go through. The hypervisor allocates the resources from the physical machine directly. With a type one, there's one layer between the Guest OS and the physical machine. VMware ESXi, Xen and KVM or type one hypervisors. KVM is a bit tricky as it looks like a type two but works like a type one. With a type two hypervisor, there are two layers. Virtualbox and VMware workstations are type two hypervisors. Also, there are multiple types of virtualization. All hypervisors support one or more of these modes and referred to them with different names. I'm going to use the Xen names for the modes and refer to KVM, VirtualBox and other hypervisors as examples. First came emulation. This is not actually virtualization
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/types-of-hypervisors?u=76281980&t=93)** as every instruction has to be translated or ambulated in software, and this is very slow. But because it's translating every instruction, you can emulate one type of CPU on another. There are some tricks like Just-In-Time emulation and caching instructions that can make it faster. However, no matter what you do, it's still very, very slow. The first virtualization was Paravirtualization or PV. This is software virtualization that does not require any virtualization support in [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md), but it's different than emulation. CPU instructions are passed directly from the virtual CPU to the real CPU, so the Guest OS sees the real CPU instead of an emulated one. As such, it processes these instructions very fast. It does, however, require the Guest Operating Systems to know they're being virtualized. Paravirtualization does not attempt to provide a complete virtual computer including a BIOS and video card. On 32-bit Intel CPUs ,this has near native performance. However, due to some architecture changes, the performance suffers on 64-bit CPUs. The Xen hypervisor supports a pure PV mode. Next came full virtualization or HVM. Full virtualization is done in hardware. As such, support for virtualization has to exist in hardware. On an Intel CPU, this is called VT-x and on AMD CPU it's called SVM. The speed of full virtualization depends on the hardware and how much is virtualized. One of the advantages is that the Guest OS doesn't need to be aware that it's being virtualized. This allows us to virtualize [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md).
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/types-of-hypervisors?u=76281980&t=186)** Full virtualization attempts to provide an entire virtual computer including BIOS and devices. It does this by using emulation for the BIOS and slower devices and ports. Type one hypervisors such as VMware, Xen and KVM support full virtualization. Also type two hypervisors such as VirtualBox take advantage of a hardware virtualization support. To speed up the slow bands we can use full virtualization with paravirtualized drivers. One of the problems with full virtualization is that the Guest OS is not aware it's being virtualized and as such cannot be optimized for the virtualized environment. The solution is to install paravirtualized drivers. This would be full virtualization with paravirtualized drivers. Paravirtualized drivers are faster as they're optimized for virtualization as opposed to generic unaware drivers. Since we can install third party drivers in Windows, this is a great way of speeding up Discs and Network Cards. Paravirtualized drivers help speed up devices that are not virtualized in hardware. However, the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the emulated devices are still slow. Paravirtualized drivers are available for most type one hypervisors. Xen alone has another mode called Hybrid Virtualization. In PVH mode, Xen dumps all emulation and accesses the hardware virtualization for everything. It uses paravirtualization for anything that isn't supported in hardware virtualization. It's about as fast as you can get. PVH looks and acts like paravirtualization, but is fast and both 32-bit and 64-bit CPUs. Because there's no emulation,
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/types-of-hypervisors?u=76281980&t=280)** the attack service is very small. There is no BIOS to exploit or virtual hardware devices that aren't needed. Amazon AWS uses PVH mode for [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) Guest by default.

#### [System images and cloud instances](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/system-images-and-cloud-instances?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/system-images-and-cloud-instances?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Male] How you initially create a VM, depends a lot on the type of virtualization you implement, as well as the scale in which you deploy. Creating one virtual machine, in a type 2 hypervisor like VirtualBox, is quite easy. You download the ISO image, create the VM, start the VM, and select the ISO image, and then install. It's not different than installing on a physical machine, however, imagine for a moment that you need to create 1000 VMs. Installing each, one at a time, interactively is not practical. The simplest way to install another VM, exactly the same as the first one, is to use a Kickstart file if you're on Red Hat, a Preseed file on Debian, or an AutoYaST file on SUSE. These will allow you to automate the install. These files either tell the installer what to do, or they answer questions that the installer would've asked a user. Either way, they allow for limited to no interaction during an install. The Red Hat installer, creates a Kickstart file when you install and saves it in /root/anaconda-ks.cfg. By passing this Kickstart file to the kernel during boot, you can replay the very same installation a second time. You can also use this Kickstart file as a template, and make changes to suit your needs. To install this way, you'd pass the location of the Kickstart file, or in the case of Debian, a Preseed file, to the kernel, and the install would be automated. To use a Kickstart file, you can include it on a USB thumb drive, a hard drive connected to the system, or even a network location, such as a web or FTP server. For instance, if we wanted to install
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/system-images-and-cloud-instances?u=76281980&t=95)** from a Kickstart file on Red Hat, we would edit the kernel boot line, and append the location. In this example, it's a web server. Using automated installation files cuts down on inconsistencies, and allows you to automate many installs. Still, the install has to happen, software has to be installed, and it all takes time. Another way is to install one VM as a golden image, and then clone it, we did this for our CentOS7 VM. If we're using one host for running [Virtual Machines](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Virtual%20Machines.md), we can clone VMs the same way as we did on VirtualBox. We shut down the VM, make one two, or three clones, and then boot them up. As we need more VMs, we can clone more until we run out of resources on the host. When we want to use a second host to run VMs, we have a problem. All of our VMs are on VM host one. To move a VM, we need to shut it down, and then export the VM in OVA, or another format, copy it across the network, and then import it, and lastly, we'd start the VM back up on VM host two. This entire process could take a really long time if the VM disk images are large. A better option is to have the VM disk images stored remotely in a storage repository or pool. The VM, and its applications would run on VM host one, but its virtual drive would be accessed from across the network. When the VM is started, it will automatically run on the VM host with the most available resources. VirtualBox isn't designed like this, but type 1 hypervisors, like VMWare ESXI, KVM, and Zen Server handle it fine. We can also migrate a running VM
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/system-images-and-cloud-instances?u=76281980&t=188)** from one VM host to another, in a matter of milliseconds because the disk image stays on the same remote storage repository. We would do this if the physical VM host was out of resources, or we needed to reboot it. In the latter case, we could move all VMs off the VM host while they're running, reboot the physical host, and move them back. Users of those VMs wouldn't even know. With this setup, the VM host machines would only need to contain, CPUs, RAM, and network cards. In a previous video, I mentioned thin provisioning. Thin provisioning gives the appearance of having more resources than are actually available. For instance, if you have 100 users, each paying for one TB, and you allocate 100 TB, that's thick provisioning, you have enough storage for everyone at full capacity. Now, if you have 100 users, each with one TB, and you allocate 10 TB, that's thin provisioning. As soon as you start running out of space, you'd allocate more storage, ideally, keeping ahead of demand. The idea is that only a small number of VMs will fill their allotted disk space, as long as you can dynamically add storage space, then there's no need to allocate it from the beginning. Thin provisioning doesn't just apply to storage either. You can thin provision CPUs, memory, or any resource. Now let's talk about bootstrapping. When creating a new instance of a VM, in a cloud setting like [Amazon Web Services](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Amazon%20Web%20Services%20(AWS).md), it's nice to inject data into the VM. We can do this using cloud-init. It's common to inject SSH keys, network configuration, and even user information. When the system boots, it looks for user data passed to it, and processes it,
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/system-images-and-cloud-instances?u=76281980&t=281)** we could even pass shell scripts as user data, if there is no solution to the configuration that we want to create. Often, you will have to manage multiple different hypervisors in one environment. When you have multiple hipervisors stacked, you end up with multiple different types of tool sets to manage them. Libvirt was created to end this. Libvirt runs on [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md), macOS, FreeBSD, and [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md). Libvirt gives us one set of tools to manage many different virtualization systems. It supports the following virtualization stacks, Xen, LXC Linux containers, OpenVZ, QEMU, VirtualBox, VMWARE ESX, WMWare Workstation, and [Microsoft](../../Instructors/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Microsoft.md) Hyper-V, among others. Libvirt doesn't just manage hypervisors either, it can manage storage. I won't cover every type of storage that it supports, but it will manage VM disk images and directories, network filesystems, iSCSI SANs, and even ZFS. Libvirt also includes the virsh command, which provides an interactive shell for managing virtualization stacks, storage, and networks. Also included are tools, such as virt-clone, used to clone VMs, virt-install, used to create the initial OS installation, virt-top, to show the resource usage of VMs, and the Virtual Machine Manager GUI. There are other commands too. You can visit the Libvirt documentation at [libvirt.org/docs.html](https://libvirt.org/docs.html), for more information.

#### [About containers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-containers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-containers?u=76281980&t=0)** - A virtual machine provides enough virtual [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) for an entire OS to run on. If the VM is para-virtualized, the hardware is minimal but is still there and will have memory, drives, and network even if the CPU is shared with the host. The operating system is complete, even if it's a bare-bones OS. In the case of virtualization, a hypervisor may run directly on bare metal. This hypervisor will then start a VM which would contain an entire operating system including an OS kernel. The operating system would contain libraries which provide a platform to run applications on. One virtual machine could run more than one application just like a real physical machine. The hypervisor could also run more than one virtual machine providing isolation between VMs. Starting these [Virtual Machines](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Virtual%20Machines.md) is much faster than starting a real physical machine, but even at best they may take anywhere from 10 to 60 seconds to boot. You can export these virtual machines to files, distribute them, and deploy them on other hosts, but because of the size of the disk image containing the entire operating system, it may take a substantial amount of time for the process to complete. Containers are a type of virtualization, but they are not virtual machines. A virtual machine focuses on providing a physical computer in software and isolating the entire OS installation. A container is different in that it is focused on an application, an application stack, or even an operating environment. Visualize a running application and the libraries that it needs. Now wrap a container around it so it is segmented from the operating system. This container application is then run on a container engine.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-containers?u=76281980&t=93)** Depending on the container engine, this may be a distributable object that can be downloaded and run. Because the container package only holds the application and its libraries, it's very small and it executes as fast as running the application outside of the container. More containers can be started and connected together, so one container may have MariaDB in it, and another has Apache with the two communicating. Non-containerized applications can also be run alongside applications in containers as well. It's important to note that performance-wise there's no difference between running an application natively on [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md), and running one in a container. You're just isolating the containerized application from other applications. You can think of containers as a sort of super chroot environment. The facilities for creating containers such as namespaces and control groups reside in the kernel. To make creating, using, and distributing containers easier, there are other software stacks such as Docker which simplifies the process. Note that both of these technologies are used to isolate applications from one another, but do it using different methods. One technology is not a replacement for the other, and in fact they work quite well together. It is likely that you will be using virtual machines running on physical machines running container engines and applications in the containers.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Virtual Machines](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Virtual%20Machines.md) (4), [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) (2), [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) (1)
> **CLI Commands:** apache (1), make (1), docker (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (2), just like (1)
> **Env Vars:** cpu (1)

#### [Introduction to cloud-init](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-cloud-init?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-cloud-init?u=76281980&t=0)** - On a small scale, it's easy to have a VM installed with an OS and manually configured. Then if you want another one you can clone it. Creating the new VM was easy. We can issue commands to the hypervisor to clone a new virtual machine whether we're using KVM, XenServer, VirtualBox or another hypervisor. Which command we use is dependent on the hypervisor so we won't spend any more time discussing that. However, once your VM infrastructure grows you'll want to have configurations or recipes that create the VMs you desire based on your needs. Maintaining a golden image for every single server type is not sustainable. The first step to creating a specific use VM on the fly would be to create a new bare VM. This would add the virtual [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) configured such as memory, disk and network card including MAC address. The next step would be to bootstrap it. This is where you provide an installation source and other necessary bits of data to make it unique. The purpose of the bootstrap step is to get the VM ready to boot and install. The last step is to start the [Configuration Management](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Configuration%20Management.md) system to finish the OS level configuration. When that's done, reboot into the new installation. Examples of configuration management systems are Puppet, Chef, SaltStack, and [Ansible](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Ansible.md). To discuss these complex systems, I would need one course each. Check the course library for more information on courses specific to the configuration management system you're interested in. For this video, we want to focus on the bootstrap process. When we initially create the VM, we usually have the ability to set some specific hardware parameters such as MAC address. This can help make the host unique when we install the OS. However, it's often necessary to inject more data
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-cloud-init?u=76281980&t=95)** into the brand new VM so we can control the boot process. For this, there's cloud-init. Cloud-init's goal's to bootstrap a cloud instance. Using cloud-init we can give a file as a parameter to the boot process for it to use. This file is called the user data file. There is a 16KB limit on the size of that file, but it can be gzipped, which can be handy for complex configurations. In this user data file we can set the host name, import SSH keys so we can log in remotely without a password on the first boot, configure software repositories so we can install software and we can actually give commands or even entire shell scripts. This opens up a lot of possibilities. You could configure the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the system with shell scripts, but it's probably better to start the configuration management system which will configure the rest of the system for you. The user data file can be in several different formats, including a file of configuration data, or a shell script. It can also be a cloud-config file. The cloud-config format can be very simple such as this script, which tells the package manager to update the software from the repositories. Or it can be much more complex. It could be hundreds of lines long, and configure many things, and then at the end set up the configuration management system to take over. The cloud-init bootstrap runs through five stages. Generator, local, network, config, and final. In the generator boot stage, systemd will decide if the cloud-init.target should be run. It does this by checking several places to see if cloud-init has been disabled. If it has not been disabled, it then starts cloud-init. In the local boot stage,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-cloud-init?u=76281980&t=188)** the cloud-init-local.service is run, which looks for local sources for cloud-init data and it starts to network. This stage blocks as much as the boot as possible, so there's no conflicts in configuration. The network stage requires the host to be online. This stage processes any user data that can be found. This is any user data that has been passed to the bootstrapping process directly or it will follow a URL to a remote user data file. It will set up disks, mount volumes, and it can also run commands. In the config stage, systemd runs cloud-config.service. The config stage runs configuration modules only. It can also run any modules that do not affect other stages of the bootstrapping. In the final stage, systemd runs cloud-final.service. The final stage runs as late as possible. In this stage we'd install operating system software. For instance we may want to install SaltStack or Ansible here. It will then start the configuration management system which will continue to configure the OS. And lastly, user scripts are also run in this stage. I realize this is a pretty quick overview of cloud-init, and there's a lot more to learn. To see the documentation for cloud-init, visit the cloud-init documentation website.

#### [Simple and compound commands](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A simple command is any of the commands you type in a regular basis. For instance, ls or grep root /etc/passwd. These are simple commands. When we refer to compound commands, things get more complex. A compound command is a couple of commands that are grouped in some way and are all executed in close succession. We have a couple ways of doing this. We can specify a second command that runs after the completion of our first command. Type clear, then type in echo "hi" ; echo "there",
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=39)** and hit Enter. This will run the first echo command and when it completes, it runs the second echo command. Note that the second echo command runs no matter whether the first command succeeds or not. This is really the same thing as typing in the first command, hitting Enter, and typing in the second command and hitting Enter again. It just queues up the command, so we don't have to wait. If you only want the second command to run if the first command succeeds, you can use the double ampersand symbols. For instance, in your home directory, type in mkdir newfolder && cd newfolder.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=76)** In this case, we only change into newfolder if its creation was successful. In our case, it was. You can see this in our prompt. Now let's go to the root directory and try the same command again. Type in cd /, and then type in clear. Now bring your line back and hit Enter. In this case, the second command doesn't run because the first wasn't successful since we couldn't make a directory in slash due to lack of permissions. If you want to execute a second command only if the first command fails, then use a double pipe symbols. While we're still on slash, let's try to create a directory again. This time type in clear, then type in mkdir newfolder || echo "directory creation failed" and hit enter. Now only when the mkdir command fails does the echo run. Now let's combine both operators. Type in clear and then type in mkdir newfolder2 && cd newfolder2
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=142)** || echo "Directory creation failed".
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=152)** In this example, it attempts to make newfolder2 and if successful it cd's into it. If it's not successful, it echo's the error message. Hit enter. Because we're still in slash and can't create a folder, we should've gotten an error message. Now let's go back to our home directory. Type in cd and hit enter. Type in clear, and then bring your line back and hit enter again. This time we should see our prompt change as the directory was created and we changed into it. These are not the only ways of creating compound commands. If we want to execute multiple commands and have them run inside a current shell, we'll use the curly braces. To demonstrate this, let's use the echo command twice. Type in clear and then type in { echo "hi" ; echo "there" ;}
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=206)** and hit enter. The output of this is similar to what we did earlier. You might be thinking this is doing exactly the same thing, but let me explain why it isn't. Let's redirect our first compound command example to a file. Type in echo "hi" ; echo "there" > hello.txt
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=232)** and hit enter. Note that hi ended up on the screen. Now let's view the text file. Type in cat hello.txt and hit enter. Only the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) there is in the file. Only the second echo got redirected to the text file. Now let's use curly braces. Bring your line back and add in the left curly brace and then after there add in a semicolon and a right curly brace and then hit enter again. Now we'll view the text file again. Type in cat hello.txt and hit enter. We can see that both lines are in this file. The curly braces force bash to process all commands inside the braces as one command. This would allow us to pipe or redirect the output. Now let's do the same thing but replace the curly braces with parenthesis. Bring your line back again and change the curly braces to a left parenthesis and a right parenthesis and hit enter again. And once again we'll view the text file. We get the same thing as using curly braces. The difference between curly braces and parenthesis is that the latter runs the commands on a sub shell. The curly braces run the commands on the same shell. Since the commands are running in a sub shell they'll have different variable scope. To show this, type in clear, then we'll type in a couple of different commands. A = 0; and for our second group, {a=10; echo "in=$a");
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=325)** for our last group, echo "out=$a".
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/simple-and-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=333)** In the first command, A is assigned to zero. Then inside the sub shell, A is assigned to 10, then we echo inside the sub shell and again outside the sub shell. Hit enter. You can see from the output that these are actually two variables named A. One in the main shell, and one in the sub shell, and they hold different values. If you want to have separate variable space, use parenthesis for a sub shell. If you just want to group commands that execute as one thing, use the curly braces.

#### [Modify the shell environment](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-the-shell-environment?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-the-shell-environment?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are two types of variables accessible in a shell session. Environmental variables are variables that are defined for the current shell and are inherited by any child processes or child shells. Shell variables are variables that are contained exclusively within the shell in which they were defined. They are often used to keep track of data, like the current working directory. To see the environmental variables, in a terminal, type in printenv and hit ENTER. If you want to get the value of an individual variable, you can specify it. Type in printenv and, for instance, type in SHELL and hit ENTER. Another way is to use the echo command to print the value by typing in echo $SHELL, and hit ENTER. To see the shell variables, we use the set command. Type in set and hit ENTER. By default, the set command shows shell functions as well as variables, which can be quite long. To change the behaviors set to POSIX mode, which doesn't show functions, use the -o POSIX option. Type in set -o posix and hit ENTER. Now type in set again and let's pipe it to less. Here we can see all of the shell variables without the shell functions. To show the difference between environmental variables and shell variables, let's set a variable in our shell. Press Q to quit and then type in VAR=TEST, and hit ENTER.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-the-shell-environment?u=76281980&t=95)** Now let's grep the output of set to view it. Type in set | grep VAR and hit ENTER. And there it is. Now let's grep the output of printenv Type in printenv | grep VAR and hit ENTER. And we can see that our variable is not a part of the environment. To turn a shell variable into an environmental variable, we can just export it. Let's export the variable we just set. Type in export VAR and hit ENTER. Verify with printenv. Type in printenv | grep VAR and hit ENTER. And we can see that our variable's now part of the environment. It isn't persistent, since we didn't save it in a file. If we rebooted, it would disappear. To unset environmental variables, we can use the export command again. Type in export -n VAR and hit ENTER. We can verify this by typing in printenv | grep VAR and hit ENTER. We can see that the variable is no longer part of the environment. To unset shell variables, we can use the unset command. Type in unset VAR and hit ENTER. Earlier, we changed a shell option by turning on POSIX mode by typing in set -o posix. We can view all shell options
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-the-shell-environment?u=76281980&t=190)** by typing in set -o and hit ENTER. To unset any shell option, use +o. For instance, to unset POSIX mode, type in set +o posix, and hit ENTER. Then we can verify this again by typing in set -o. We can change the value of environment and shell variables by setting them. We can also change bash options using the set command. However, these values only survive for the current login session. To make them persistent, we need to add them to one of the bash startup files. Bash stores its configuration settings in multiple startup files. Different files are processed depending on how the shell starts. When we log in to [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) by typing in a password, Linux first executes the etc profile, which in turn executes scripts in /etc/profile/.d. These two items are the systemwide filing directories holding our environment and shell variables. Then bash executes the ._profile in the user's home directory, which holds environment and shell variables specific to the user. The local .bash_profile in turn executes the local .bashrc file, which holds aliases and bash functions. In CentOS, the local.bashrc also executes the systemwide etc bashrc file, which holds systemwide aliases and functions.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-the-shell-environment?u=76281980&t=284)** When that is complete, it returns control back to the local .bashrc file in the user's home directory. The local .bashrc file holds aliases and functions specific to the user. Once all of this is done, the user logs in. If a user is already logged and executes a shell script, it's executed in a shell, but it's not a login shell. Also, if a user is logged into the GUI and starts a shell, it's not a login shell. Generally speaking, a login shell requires entering a password or providing a login key. For non-login shells, only the user's .bashrc gets run. Again, on CentOS, the local .bashrc file executes the global etc bashrc file, which holds systemwide aliases and functions. Then it returns control back to the local .bashrc file again and finishes processing it. This file should contain aliases and functions specific to the user. Once this is all done, the shell starts. In summary, the various bash profile files are for environmental and shell variables useful for interactive logins. If you want to set a variable for all users, set it in either the etc profile or a script in etc profile.d. If you want to set a variable specific to a user, change it in the user's .bash_profile. If you want to add aliases or functions for all users, add them to the etc/bashrc.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-the-shell-environment?u=76281980&t=380)** If you want to add aliases or functions specific to a user, add them to the user's .bashrc. It's important to note that when you add aliases or functions to the user's .bashrc file, be sure to add them to the end of the file after etc/bashrc has been called to override systemwide aliases and functions.

#### [Command history](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/command-history?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/command-history?u=76281980&t=0)** - Using the shell's history can keep you from having to type in commands over again. If you want to just bring up the last command you may want to use your up and down arrow keys. If you want to see what commands you've typed in use the history command by typing in 'history' into a shell. History shows the most recent commands we've typed up to the shell limit. The limit is usually 500 or 1,000 items depending on the configuration. If you want to bring back the entire last line from the history, type in '!!' and hit enter. In my case, it's the history command which we just typed in. If you want to execute the command four lines from the bottom, you'll use '!-4' and hit enter. Type in 'history' again to see the list. We can see that four lines from the bottom was the clear command, for me, for you it may be something else. If you want to run a specific line, just specify the number after the exclamation point. For instance, to run the command on line seven, you'd type in '!7' and hit enter. If you do not have a seventh item, choose a different number in your history list. You can also run the lines starting with something. For instance, to run the most recent line that starts with 'LS' type in '!LS' and hit enter. If you want to run a command with the previous lines arguments and options you can append '!*' to the command name. To set this up, let's type in 'LS /var/log' and hit enter. To CD to the /var/log directory in our last command we can type in 'cd !*' and hit enter.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/command-history?u=76281980&t=98)** Now let's type in 'history' to see what happened. According to the history command we did 'ls /var/log' followed up by 'cd /var/log' when in reality we typed in 'cd !*'. If you want to insert the entire previous line of our history on the command prompt we can use '!!'. To set this up, let's try to view a file without proper permissions. Type in 'cat /etc/shadow' and hit enter, and it gives us a permission denied message. Append the entire previous line after sudo using '!!' by typing in 'sudo !!'. This will take the entire previous line which is cat /etc/shadow and append it to the end of this line. Hit enter, type in your password and we can see that it worked. Type in 'history' again to see what was done. You can see that we typed in 'cat /etc/shadow' then the 'sudo !!' ended up executing sudo cat /etc/shadow. The negative to these commands is that we need to know where in the history we typed the command and thus have to type the history command first. A faster way to search through previous commands is using the 'reverse-i-search'. Press control + r and you'll see your prompt changes to reverse-i-search. Now type in 'LS' and you'll see that the last line that starts with LS shows up. If you want to run this line, press enter.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/command-history?u=76281980&t=191)** If you'd like to edit the line before running it press the right arrow key or left arrow key. If you want to step through a reverse-i-search, just press control + r again. For instance, press control + r, type in 'LS', and then if you want to go to the next item press control + r again. When you get to the one that you want, hit enter.
