#### [The PATH and command execution](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/the-path-and-command-execution?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/the-path-and-command-execution?u=76281980&t=0)** Bash has some pre-specified locations to look for commands in. These locations are stored in a variable called path. When you type in a command, Bash will look for the command in each of the directories in the path variable and execute the first one it finds. This means if you have the same command in more than one directory, the one that it finds first will get executed. We can look at the path variable using the echo command. In a terminal type in echo, space, dollar sign, capital P-A-T-H, and hit enter. Each directory has been limited by a colon and Bash looks from the left to the right. This variable will have different contents depending on the user, and the users can add directories to their copy as well. If you want to search for a command in the path without using the more complex find or locate commands, just use the which command. Type in which, space, and then the command. In this case I'll use tar. And hit enter. This'll tell you which direct your tar is in if it's in your path. If you want to get more data including the man pages for the command, use the whereis command. Type in whereis, space, tar and hit enter. To add a directory to the path variable, you need to put it in one of your shell startup scripts. Let's create a directory called scripts and add it to our path variable. Type in clear and then type in mkdir, space, scripts and hit enter. Now let's add it to our dot bashrc file. Type in vi, space, tilde, slash, dot bashrc. And hit enter. Go into insert mode by pressing the I key and go down to the bottom. We're going to add two lines. Type in capital P-A-T-H, equals, dollar sign, capital P-A-T-H, colon, tilde, slash, scripts.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/the-path-and-command-execution?u=76281980&t=96)** Then hit enter and type export, space, capital P-A-T-H. Note that we're signing the path variable with the current contents of the path variable plus our scripts directory. Also note that we didn't include the dollar sign when exporting the variable. Now save and exit by pressing escape, colon, x, exclamation mark and hitting enter. Note this script hasn't run yet, so you'll need to log on back in again. A quicker solution for our current session is to source the file. Type in source, space, tilde, slash dot bashrc, then hit enter. This will process the file immediately. Let's check the path variable again. Type in echo, space, dollar sign, capital P-A-T-H, and hit enter. And we can see our scripts directory at the end. You will definitely want to log out and back in to make sure it works on a new shell as well. If you're an administrator and you want to add a directory to every users path variable, you'll need to do it at a system level. This means adding it to the etc profile or, even better, adding a script to the slash, etc, slash, profile, dot d directory. Let's do that. Type in clear then type in sudo, space, vi, space slash etc, slash profile dot d, slash scripts dot s-h. This file doesn't exist yet, but vi will create it. We can name it anything we want, but make sure it has a dot s-h extension. Hit enter. Enter your password if prompted. Go into insert mode and add exactly the same lines as we did before. Capital P-A-T-H, equals, dollar sign, capital P-A-T-H, colon, tilde, slash, scripts. Then hit enter and type in export, space, capital P-A-T-H.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/the-path-and-command-execution?u=76281980&t=194)** And then exit by pressing escape, colon, x, exclamation mark, and hitting enter. Because we're using the tilde shortcut for the users home directory, it should work for every user, although if a new user wants to take advantage of this, they'll need to create the script directory in their home.

#### [Named and unnamed pipes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/named-and-unnamed-pipes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/named-and-unnamed-pipes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A pipe is a way of one program communicating with another. A common use for a pipe is taking the output of one command and sending it to the input of another command. For instance, we could grap a file and if the output was too long to fit on the screen, we could pipe the output to less a pager. Type into a terminal grap space TCP space slash ETC slash services space pipe space less, and hit enter. This looks through the ETC services file for the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) TCP and then sends the output to less which presents it one page at a time. Press Q to quit. You can pipe any number of times, as it's not limited to just two commands. For example, type in grap space TCP space slash ETC services space pipe space AWK space single quote left curly bracket print space dollar sign one right curly bracket single quote space pipe space sort space pipe space less. This searches the ETC services file for the word TCP and then sends the lines to awk, which prints out the first column only, then sends the remaining output to sort, which sorts it, and finally sends it to less to be one page at a time. Hit enter. The type of pipes we are using are considered unnamed pipes. An unnamed pipe is a direct connection between two commands running in the same terminal.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/named-and-unnamed-pipes?u=76281980&t=95)** If we want to send output from a command in one terminal to another command in a different terminal, we can use a named pipe, or FIFO. FIFO stands for first in, first out. This is a pipe that exists in the file system. The pipe acts like a file on a disk that one process can write to while another reads from. A named pipe is still a direct connection between programs that just uses the file system as an interface. When one process is writing to the named pipe, its output gets blocked until the receiving process starts reading it. Let's take a look at how this works. To set this up, let's open a second terminal by going to our file menu and down to open terminal. This terminal we're going to drag to the right and bump the font size. I'll drag the original terminal to the left so it fills up the remaining space. Press Q to get out of less. Now we're going to create our named pipe. In the left-hand terminal, type in MK FIFO space named underscore pipe, and hit enter. This makes a pipe called named underscore pipe. This name is arbitrary. You can choose a different name if you wish. Now let's send some data to this pipe using echo in a redirect. Type in echo space double quote hi double quote space greater than space named pipe, and hit enter. Notice that I'm in my home directory in both of these terminals. Also notice that we didn't get our terminal bent. This is because the IO is being blocked. The echo command can't finish printing the word hi until another process reads it from the named pipe.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/named-and-unnamed-pipes?u=76281980&t=191)** Now in the second terminal, let's use cat to read the pipe. Type in cat space named pipe, and hit enter. We see that as soon as cat reads the output from the named pipe, the echo command was able to finish. We can use named pipes to send output from one command in the OS to another command without them running in the same shell or even by the same user. We don't need our second terminal anymore, so close it and make the first one full screen. Now let's take a look at the [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md) of the named pipe using LS. Type in LS space dash L, and hit enter. You'll notice that the left-hand column, we can see that the leftmost character is a P for pipe. To get rid of a named pipe, you can delete it like any other file. Type in RM space named pipe, and hit Enter.

#### [Use input-output redirection (>, >>, |, 2>, etc.)](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/2273099?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/2273099?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Every command has one input, standard in, and two outputs, standard out, and standard error. A pipe sends standard out from one command to the standard in of another. A redirect works in a similar, manner, but the source of destination is the file system. By default, the output of a command goes to the screen. This includes standard out, and standard error. Standard out is the successful output from a command, standard error is used for error messages. Redirection allows us to split these two outputs individually. We can redirect either output, or both to a disk. Commands also have a standard in. If you wanted to redirect a file to a command, we can with a redirect toward the command. Once the command has processed the output, we could then redirect that output back to the disc. Let's see what this looks like using commands. To redirect standard out from a command to a file, use a greater than symbol. One greater than symbol overwrites the file, or creates it if it doesn't exist. To append to a file, supply two greater than symbols. This will also create the file if it doesn't exist. If standard out is the first output, then standard error must be the second output. To redirect standard error, supply two greater than. Again, this overwrites the file if it exists, or creates it if it doesn't. To append to an existing file, redirect the standard error with two greater than, greater than. There are times when we might want to redirect all output from a command to a file. To do this, we'll use ampersand greater than.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/2273099?u=76281980&t=96)** Note that if we redirect all output from a command, nothing will show up on the screen. Once again, to append, add an extra greater than symbol. You can redirect both into the standard end of a command, and then back out to a file again. For instance, we can redirect a file into the sort command to sort it, and then redirect the sorted output back out to a different file. We won't be able to redirect back over the original file though, as it will conflict. There are times where we might want to send the output of a command to a file, and to the screen. Neither pipes or redirects handle this natively, but [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) provides a special command for doing this, called tee. It splits, or tees the output, and sends it both to a file, and to the screen. This example takes the output of ls, and saves it to a file named lsout.txt, as well as display it on the screen. If we want to append to the lsout.txt file, we can add the -a, or append option to tee. Let's see how this works by using redirects in a terminal. In a terminal, type in find /etc | sort | tee etcsort.txt | wc-l.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/2273099?u=76281980&t=177)** In this example, we get a list of all files in etc using the find command. That list will send to the sort command using a pipe. We'll split the output by using the tee command to save a copy of the sorted list to a file called etcsort.txt, but also send it into the next pipe. Lastly, we'll use wc -l to count the lines. Go ahead and hit enter. What we should see when done is the sorted lines in the etcsort.txt file, and the number of lines on the screen. Notice the last line is the output from wc-l, and is the number of lines in the file. Also notice that we have more than just the number of lines on the screen. We have what looks like permission denied error messages from the find command. We can redirect standard error using the two greater than symbols. Let's add this. Press the up arrow key, and move your cursor over to etc, and insert 2> etcerr.txt.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/2273099?u=76281980&t=236)** You should have find /etc 2> etcerr.txt | sort | tee etcsort.txt | wc-l, and hit enter. Now the error messages from find will be saved in etcerr.txt. The non-error message text will be sorted, and saved as etcsort.txt. And the total lines will be displayed on screen. One last tip I'd like to share is totally getting rid of output from a command by redirecting it to a bottomless pit. This bottomless pit is provided by /dev/null. If we redirect output from commands to /dev/null, nothing will show on the screen. For instance, type in find /etc &> /dev/null, and hit enter.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/2273099?u=76281980&t=293)** All output disappeared. Whenever we want to hide any output from a command, we can just direct it to /dev/null.

One thing to remember about cat is that it will display anything, even binary files without warning. Cat will display a file and keep doing it until it gets to the end. If you want to display that file one page at a time you can use less. There's an older tool called more that will do the same thing but is less powerful. We have similar commands to view the beginning and end of a file called head and tail. These are very fast as they don't read the entire file into memory. #### [Find files using locate](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-files-using-locate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-files-using-locate?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) directory structure is deep and has many files. Knowing how to find those files is important. Linux has two different commands for finding files, locate and find. The locate command uses a database created by the update db command. Being database driven, it's very fast but only contains files that are listed in the locate database. By default in CentOS the locate database is updated once per day by a system service. Let's search for the file name bzip2. using the locate command. In a terminal type in locate a space bzip2 and hit Enter. This will search the locate database and find files that include the name bzip2. We can count the number of files by adding the dash c option. Bring your line back and add dash c and hit Enter. We can see that we get 24 results. If you want to search for more than one file just add it to the line. Type in clear, type in locate space, bzip2 space man and hit Enter. This gives a lot more results. Let's count them. Bring your line back again and add dash c. We can see that we get a lot more results. Just adding the additional search criteria searches for any file that has the words bzip2 or man. If you want to search for bzp2 and man you need to use a dash capital A option. Type in clear then bring your line back and change dash c to dash capital A and hit Enter. You can see this gives us only two results. In Linux file names are case sensitive. If you want to include both lower and upper case results past the dash I option for case insensitivity.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-files-using-locate?u=76281980&t=95)** For instance, type in locate space dash i space high and hit Enter. As you can see, with this option, we get both upper and lowercase results. By default, the locate command uses wildcards to match patterns. This would be wildcards like the asterisk symbol that matches any number of any characters. For instance, type in clear and type in locate space asterisk dot png and hit Enter. This will show all files in the database ending in dot png. Wildcards are useful, but if you want more powerful pattern matching, locate also supports basic and extended regular expressions. It's followed by dot asterisk which matches any number of any character. This is the same as the asterisk wildcard and fall globs. In this example, locate uses the dash s regex option which will use an extended regular expression to show all file paths that start with slash usr includes pixmaps or backgrounds in the middle and ends with jpg or png. This is a bit more complex than just including two things to search for, but will give exactly what we want. Hit Enter. Regular expressions are more complex and file wildcards but more powerful. See the pattern matching chapter this course for more examples on how to use regular expressions. #### [Finding files with find](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-files-with-find?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-files-with-find?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Grant] [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) includes both the locate and find commands for searching the file systems. Locate is database driven and find is not, so it's slower but the results are always up to date and it's much more powerful. Although the syntax can be difficult. Let's start by using the find command to find a file by name. Unlike locate find requires a search path. Do you want to search the entire file system, just use slash as the path. We also need to elevate privileges using sudo. Type in sudo space find space slash space dash name space bzip2 and hit enter. This will search the entire file system for a file named bzip2. We get three results. We have to elevate privileges with sudo or we can't access certain files. Let's do the same search with locate. Type in locate space dash c to count space bzip2 and hit enter. The locate command found 24 files matching the name bzip2. Find only finds three. This is because locate is not very strict and finds files and directories with bzip2 anywhere in the path. By default find only searches for the exact characters you've entered. To get find to act more like locate, we have to use wildcards. Type in clear and then type in sudo space find space slash space dash names space asterisk bzip2 asterisk and hit enter. This runs the find command, searches through the entire file system looking for anything with bzip2 in the name. Compare this with the output of locate and you'll find it's a lot closer. Find however has a great deal more power than locate. So let's take a look at some of those features.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-files-with-find?u=76281980&t=95)** We can use find to search for files that belong to a certain user. Type in clear, then type in sudo space find space slash space dash u s e r space and then your username. In my case that's Grant. And hit enter. We can also find by group. Type in clear and then type in sudo space find space slash space dash group space and for instance we use the audio group. And hit enter. If you get warnings, it's because find is searching through virtual file systems and since those files don't actually exist on the disk, it may give you error messages. You can ignore these. We can also use find to search for files based on size. This may be useful if you're trying to free up disk space and need to find your largest files. Type in clear and then type in sudo space find space slash space dash size space plus one capital m. This finds files on the entire file system larger than one Megabyte. An interesting capability to find is that it can execute another command on each item in the search results. Type in clear, then bring your line back and add space dash exec space stat space dash c space double quote percent s space percent n double quote space left curly bracket right curly bracket space backslash semicolon. This example is much more complex but what it does is use find to search for files in the entire file system larger than one Megabyte, then find runs the stat command which outputs the size and name for each file.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-files-with-find?u=76281980&t=190)** The curly brackets are the place holder for each search items name. The backslash semicolon tells find that there are no more commands to run. Go ahead and hit enter. If you want to sort the files smallest to largest, pipe the output to sort. To get a proper numeric sort, we'll want to use the dash n option. Type in clear, then bring your line back and add space pipe space sort space dash n and hit enter again. Here we see the largest files at the bottom. We can also search for filed based on when the file was accessed, modified or the data attributes changed. Let's say we saved a file to the disk but don't remember where we put it and don't remember the name. It would be beneficial to find files modified in less than one day. Type in clear, then type in sudo space find space slash space dash mtime space dash one and hit enter. If you wanted to find files that have not been modified in the last day, use plus one. Bring your line back and change dash one to plus one. And hit enter again. If you only want to search for files but not directories, we can provide the dash type option. Type in clear. Type in sudo space find space slash home space dash type space f. This will show a list of files inside the slash home directory. Hit enter. If you wanted a list of directories, we could use dash type space d. Type in clear, bring your line back and change dash type space f to dash type space d and hit enter again. Find is a very, very powerful tool
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-files-with-find?u=76281980&t=283)** and I'd suggest checking out the man page as we've only scratched the surface.

#### [Finding text in files with grep](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The most efficient way of searching for data in a file is to use grep. Grep shows lines in the file that match the provided search criteria. The syntax is grep followed by options and then the search criteria and lastly the name of the file to search to. Grep has many many options, but the ones I like to use most are -i for case insensitive searches,
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980&t=18)** - v for inverted searches, which shows the opposite of the search criteria,
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980&t=22)** - c for the number of lines that matched,
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980&t=24)** - o to show only characters that match not the entire line,
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980&t=28)** - r to recursively grep, this searches through all files in a directory, and -E to use extended regular expressions, this is the same as using egrep. There are many many more options. View the grep man page for more information. This example of using options shows a case insensitive search for the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) root in /etc/password. You can also pipe the output of any command into grep. In this example I'm using the find command to search for files in the entire operating system with names ending with .txt. This output is then piped into grep and it searches for the word apache. This is a very common use for grep. To make our search criteria more strict we can employ anchors. The caret anchor forces grep to search from the beginning of the line. The dollar sign anchors to the end. A search criteria of ^$ might not seem that useful. It will anchor to the beginning and the end, but will no match anything in the middle. This very simple criteria finds blank lines. You might wonder why we'd want to do that, but if you combine it with a -v invert option it will only show non-blank lines, it's very clever. We have criteria for matching characters. To match one character of any type we use the dot. To match more characters we use a modifier. The asterisk is a common modifier. It says to match zero or more of the previous character. For instance, we use a dot which matches any one character and follow it with a asterisk modifier which matches zero or more of any previous characters. The result is that we match any number of any characters. This is equivalent to the asterisk by itself in file globs. Character sets match exactly one character just like the dot
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980&t=123)** but we can be specific about which character to match and include a list of possible characters, such as a, b, c. The one character that matches can either be a, b, or c. We can also contain ranges of characters, such as zero through nine or a through z. Just remember that the set will only match one character, but that one character can be any of the items in the list. To match everything except what is in the character set make sure the first character is an exclamation mark. This will negate the match. A character class is a reliable posix compliant way of matching certain types of characters, such as numbers, upper and lower case letters, or both at the same time as well as the same including numbers. They can also match spaces alone, which include tabs and new lines. They can match printable characters, not including spaces, printable characters including spaces, punctuation, and even non-printable control characters, or hexadecimal numbers. These are very difficult to match using standard sets. You can place character classes in side of sets. So user [0-9] is the same as user \[\[:digit:]] and either will match user zero, user one, user two, and so on up to user nine. You can also add more than one character class inside of a set. In this example it will match user zero through nine or user with a trailing space but no number. Negating a character class is exactly the same as negating items in a set. I've included it because it looks odd. Just place the exclamation mark
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980&t=216)** right before the character class and it will negate the character class. In this example it will find user followed by anything but a digit. There are two types of regular expressions, basic and extended. Extended regular expressions do everything that basic regular expressions do and I recommend using them whenever possible as the syntax is actually simpler than basic regular expressions. Not every tool supports them, but you can use them with sed -r, egrep, awk, and bash has a built in regular expression operator. In brief one dot matches one character of any type just like before. An asterisk modifier matches zero or more of the previous character. A question mark modifier matches zero or one of the previous character. And a plus modifier matches one or more of the previous character. If you want to specify exactly how many matches we can place a digit inside of curly braces. For instance, for two of the previous character we can place a two inside of curly braces. You can do this with basic regular expressions with GNU tools, but you need to escape the curly braces with back slashes. To specify a range such as two through four of the previous character we can use 2,4 in the curly braces. We can also group matches if we want to match on more than one character. For instance, if you wanted to match more than one occurrence of the characters ab together. We could follow this with a modifier to specify the number of occurrences. This would match two occurrences of ab, such as ab, ab. Extended regular expressions support alternation by creating a group and placing two items in it separated by a pipe we can match cat or dog. This is really the one big advantage of extended
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/finding-text-in-files-with-grep?u=76281980&t=311)** over basic regular expressions. Let's look at an example. The caret anchors to the beginning of the line and will only show lines that start with http. The dot matches any one character and the asterisk after it modifies it to any number of characters. So .* will match any number of any characters. The letters tcp are literal. The .* again matches any number of any characters and lastly the word service followed by the dollar sign anchors to the end. This will only match lines that end with the word service. Using grep-E or egrep, we can use alternation and we could match both tcp and udp by creating a group with both patterns separated by a pipe. This will match either tcp or udp in this line.

The first tool we'll look at to monitor processes is the PS command. In a terminal type in PS and hit enter. By default PS only shows the processes run by the user executing it. It shows the process ID, the terminal it was run on, the aggregated execution time, and the command that was run. Oddly, the PS command has three different types of syntax options; unix, BSD, and GNU. The unix options look as you would expect with a dash before a single letter. The GNU options are words with two dashes just like other commands. The odd ones are the BSD options which don't have any dashes at all. For this course, we'll focus on the unix and GNU options. To get PS to display every process we'll want to use the dash E option. Type in PS space dash E and hit enter. This still shows the same columns as PS with no options but it shows every process. To give us an idea which processes started other processes We can pass the hierarchy option, or dash capital H. Type in PS space dash E capital H. We can see what's in dentation which processes our child processes. You can add this option to any others that we'll cover in this course. To show more information about all processes add the dash F or full option. Type in PS space dash E lowercase F and hit enter. This adds the username who ran the command,

This is usually my go to set of options for PS. If you want more information we can change the lowercase F to an uppercase F. Type in PS space dash E uppercase F and hit enter. This adds allocated memory, the total memory size, and the CPU the process is running on. If that's not enough you may want to add the dash L for long format. In a terminal, start top by typing in T O P and hitting enter. Note that the display is being updated every couple of seconds. In the summary area at the top of the screen, top shows how long the system has been up, the load average, how many processes are running, how many are waiting for CPU time, the overall CPU usage and memory usage. One of those signals would be terminate if you wanted to end the process. If that doesn't work, we can forcibly kill a process by sending a kill signal. First, though, we need to know the process ID. We've already talked about the PS command for this, but we have a couple of other tools that are simpler. In a terminal type in pidof space cron D and hit enter. Also type in pgrep, space, cron D, and hit enter. Pgrep is more powerful and allows us to filter on a lot of items like username and command. But for the purpose of just getting a process ID, either will work fine. Once we have the process ID, we can use the kill command to send out a signal. To list all possible signal types, type in kill space dash L and hit enter. There are 64 different signals, but it's not common practice to use all of them. Generally, we want to send the process a non destructive signal like SIGHUB or SIG User one, or we want to terminate a process with SIGTERM or SIGKILL. In many cases, SIGHUB forces a process to reread as configuration file. Read the man pages for the process and question to see more details. #### [Run a program with higher or lower priority than the default](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/run-a-program-with-higher-or-lower-priority-than-the-default?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/run-a-program-with-higher-or-lower-priority-than-the-default?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) process priorities are handled by a system called nice. Nice levels go from negative one, to negative 19 for privileged users. The scale goes from zero to 19 for non privileged users. The default nice level is zero. When a process is started it has a default nice level. The nice level is a number that reflects how nice the process is to the CPU. The nicer the process is to the CPU, the less it interrupts it, and the lower its priority is. Because the process is getting fewer resources from the CPU, it also has lower priority. This means other processes will get more CPU time than it will. If the nice level is less than zero, than it is less nice to the CPU, and demands more resources from it, thus having a higher priority for CPU time. In summary, the higher the nice number, the lower the CPU priority. The lower the nice number, the higher the CPU priority. Let's go to terminal to see how this works. If you want to start a process with a predetermined nice level, we can start it with a nice command. I'm going to open a second terminal by going to File, Open Terminal, and drag it to the right border, and then bump the font size. I'm going to take my original terminal and double click the title bar, and then drag it to the left border. Now I can see both terminals. In the right hand terminal, type in nice space dash two space top, and hit enter. In the left terminal, type in PS space dash capital C

Notice the nice level is positive two instead of negative 2. The argument is that the nice level is hyphen followed by the numeric nice level, thus hyphen two equals two. Click on the right hand terminal and press Q to quit top. If you want to set a nice level of negative two, the option would be hyphen hyphen two. Also, only privileged users can set negative nice levels, so we'll need to use sudo. Type in sudo space nice space hyphen hyphen two space top, and hit enter. Type in your password and hit enter again. Now in the first terminal, bring your line back and hit enter. Now you see that top is running with a nice level of negative two. The niceness of a process can also be changed after the process is running using the renice command. We'll need our process ID number for the next command. Type in sudo space renice space five space and then the process ID from the previous command, in my case it's four seven seven two, then hit enter. Type in your password and hit enter again. #### [Understand the differences between basic and extended regular expressions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/understand-the-differences-between-basic-and-extended-regular-expressions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/understand-the-differences-between-basic-and-extended-regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=0)** - Within the POSIX standard, there are two different types of regular expressions. Basic, or BREs, and extended, or EREs. BREs came out first. EREs were extended and had more power. Historically, there were differences, but over time, the features have merged until the functionality is mostly the same. Basic regular expressions are no longer basic and extended are no longer more advanced. Most tutorials show basic regular expressions first, as that seems logical, but, in fact, extended regular expressions are cleaner and easier to use. So, we'll start with them. Let's talk about the ERE Syntax. In an extended regular expression, the dot, square brackets, backslash, parentheses, and pipe symbol are special characters with special functionality. The dot matches one character. The square brackets are used for character sets. The backslash is used to escape single characters. And, the left parentheses matches up with the right parentheses to provide pattern grouping. Lastly, the pipe symbol provides alternation. The asterisk symbol, plus symbol, question mark, and left curly brace are also special characters. They are repetition operators and reference the preceding character. If they appear first, they are not special, as there are no previous characters to modify. The circumflex or caret and the dollar sign are also special characters. The circumflex anchors to the beginning as a line, and the dollar sign anchors to the end. Inside square brackets, the circumflex negates the pattern if it's the first character, so it gains a different special status. If we need any of these special characters to match themselves, we need to include them in square brackets or escape them with a backslash. So, with EREs, escaping special characters makes them lose their special functionality. Now, let's contrast this with basic regular expressions.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/understand-the-differences-between-basic-and-extended-regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=95)** In BREs, there are fewer special characters. The question mark, plus symbol, left and right curly braces, pipe symbol, and left and right parentheses lose their special status. Since these characters are no longer special, they get processed by the shelves instead of regular expression by the tools. To make them special, we need to escape them with backslashes. In BREs, escaping them makes them special. This leaves the dot, square brackets, backslash, and asterisk as special characters. Let me show you why you should be concerned with this and how it affects you. Let's say we have a string of A-B-A-B-A-B-C-C-C-C-C-C-D, and we use an extended regular expression of left parentheses, A-B, right parentheses. The parentheses creates a group and matches it. This would match A-B-A-B-A-B. To do the same thing in a basic regular expression, we'd need to escape the parentheses. The equivalent regular expression would be backslash, left parentheses, A-B, backslash, right parentheses. Let me give you one more. Using the same text, lets match two instances of of A-B and/or zero or one instance of C-D. This pattern with an ERE would be left parentheses, A-B, right parentheses, left curly bracket, two, right curly bracket, pipe, left parentheses, C-D, right parentheses, left curly bracket, comma one, right curly bracket. With a BRE, the syntax is quite a lot more complex. All of the parentheses, curly braces, and pipe symbol need to be escaped with backslashes. Are you convinced yet, that you should use EREs where ever possible? They are easier to read and easier to create. As per the POSIX standard,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/understand-the-differences-between-basic-and-extended-regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=188)** BREs and EREs are almost identical. BREs cannot to alteration in the POSIX standard; however, in using GNU tools, GNU BREs can do anything GNU EREs can do and vice versa. I can't stress this enough, they are equal with GNU tools. In non-GNU tools, this may not be the case. For this course, we'll be focusing on GNU EREs, as the syntax is cleaner and will only revert to BREs if we have no other choice. By default, some tools automatically assume BREs. We'll talk about that next.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/matching-characters-and-words-with-regexes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We're going to start our discussion on extended regular expressions by talking about how to match single characters and words. To match one character of any type except null, we use a dot. To specify which character to match while escaping it, we use a character set with square brackets. If you haven't watched the video on the effective locale on searches, you should do so now. This will explain some very strange behavior with characters set ranges. If we want to match A, B, or C, we'll do left square bracket ABC right square bracket. Note that it will only match one character and that character can be A, B or C. If you want to negate the match, for instance, we want to match one character but it cannot be A, B, or C, we proceed the match with the circumflex. You can do ranges with character sets too, for numbers, this is safe, for letters it's best to use a character class. The POSIX we have matching different types of characters is to use a character class. This character class alpha matches any upper or lowercase letter. GNU EREs support special expressions using backslash characters followed by regular characters. For instance, backslash B will match the empty string at either edge of a [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md). For example, backslash B rat backslash B matches the separate word rat, but not brat, drat or rattle. This might seem like the same thing as using a space character class, which would also match rat but not brat, drat, or rattle. However, it is not the same. The space character class matches and includes the space in the output, whereas the backslash B matches the empty string but does not include the space and the result. backslash capital B will match an empty string
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/matching-characters-and-words-with-regexes?u=76281980&t=94)** as long as it's not at the edge of a word. For instance, backslash capital B rat, backslash capital B, matches crate, but not furry rat because the empty string immediately precedes the letter R. Again, it seems like the same thing to use a character class and match anything but a space character, but the character class matches a non-space character before and after rat and includes them in the output. In this case, the letters C and E, whereas the backslash capital B does not. Backslash less than matches the empty string at the beginning of a word, whereas backslash greater than matches the empty string at the end of a word, backslash W matches whole words. For instance, backslash W rat would match crate and crateandbarrel. It matches the rat and then includes the entire word. It has a synonym for left square bracket, underscore, left square bracket, colon, all num, colon, right square bracket, right square bracket in a character class. Backslash capital W matches non-words. It is a synonym for the previous character class, but negated. Backslash S matches white space, it is the same as the space character class. Backslash capital S matches non-white space. It is the opposite of backslash S, and is a synonym for a negative space character class. I'm going to go ahead and add anchors to this table. If you want to match the beginning of a line just prepend the pattern with a caret or circumflex. If you want to anchor the pattern to the end of the line, append a dollar sign to the pattern.

#### [Specifying occurrences with regexes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/specifying-occurrences-with-regexes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/specifying-occurrences-with-regexes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If we only had to match single characters we'd be done discussing regular expressions. But we often have to specify occurrences of characters. We talked a bit about this when we discussed extended globs. We have more power with regular expressions because we can be specific. We've already talked about how to match one character. We use a dot if we don't want to be specific about what type of character it is. If we want to specify, we can use character sets, character classes, or even just specify the character itself, such as a letter or a number. I will use the dot for these examples. If I want to match zero or one of any character I'd specify a question mark after the dot. This specifies how many of the previous character to match. In my case, I'm using a dot for my previous character, so it will match zero or one of any character. For instance, dog.? would match dog and dogs, as dog would be zero occurrences of the preceding character, and dogs would be one occurrence. To match one or more of the preceding character, use dot plus to combine question mark, which is zero one, and plus, which is one or more, we'd use dot asterisk, which is zero or more occurrences of the preceding character. To specify the number of occurrences, we'd put the number inside curly braces. To match the previous character exactly three times, we'd use .{3}. If we want to match three or more, we'd add a comma but no ending number, so .{3,} would match three or more. If you want to match zero to three occurrences, we'd use the comma again, but before the three.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/specifying-occurrences-with-regexes?u=76281980&t=93)** Since we're not specifying the fewest matches, it assumes zero. By specifying the fewest and the most occurrences we have a range. This would match one to three occurrences. We cannot match one three and five occurrences at the same time without alternation, which we'll talk about next. Note that GNU BREs do support curly braces to specify occurrences, but you need to escape them using backslashes.

#### [Alternation and grouping with regexes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/alternation-and-grouping-with-regexes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/alternation-and-grouping-with-regexes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Alternation allows us to specify one thing or another. Grouping allows combining patterns into one match. This is important because we can use alternation to match one or another group, or we can use a occurrence operands to specify how many times we should match the entire group. This might not seem like it's that important, but using alternation expression groups and specifying occurrences can be quite powerful. Let's start with the basic example. To specify dog or cat separate them with a pipe symbol. This would match both dog and cat or even multiple occurrences of both if we didn't specify an anchor or occurrence operands due to concatenation and greedy matches. However, this syntax is the foundation of alternation. We want to specify the number of occurrences. We place the regex inside of a parenthesis and then specify an occurrence operand. For instance, if you wanted to match zero or one dog or cat, we'd use caret, left parenthesis, dog, pipe, cat, right parenthesis, question mark, dollar sign. Notice I added anchors. Otherwise the ERE will do a greeting match and will match too many times. This would match zero or one occurrences of either dog or cat, but not more occurrences. Just by changing the question mark to a plus symbol, it will match one or more occurrences. Now let's try to match numbers between one and 99. We need to accommodate for single digits. To match zero through nine we'll start with a character set in a range of zero through nine. If we just added a second range of numbers then it will match 10 through 99, but not zero through nine. So we need to use alternation to match both single digits and double. We could also use an occurrence operand instead of alternation to satisfy our requirements
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/alternation-and-grouping-with-regexes?u=76281980&t=92)** with a shorter regular expression. The first set is required and matches zero through nine. The second character set also matches zero through nine, but the occurrence operand after the second set specifies zero or one of the previous. With only the first set it will match zero through nine and combined with the second set it will match zero through 99. Let's take this a step further. Matching zero to 99 is pretty easy, because it's the maximum value allowed by the number of digits. Let's match numbers zero through 150. So we need the regular expression that matches zero through nine, which we can use a single character class for. Then we need to match 10 through 99. So we need another character class and an occurrence operand similar to what we just did. Zero to 150 is more complex. We're wanting to match from zero to 150, but if we allow one in the first column and zero through five in the second column we'll still need to allow zero through nine in the third column to match 149, but this will also match 159, which is more than what we want. So we're going to remove 150 and only match 100 through 149 with this pattern. This will be easier. Lastly we'll match 150 by itself. Let's start by taking care of zero through 99 since we've already done this. This matches zero through 99. Now let's take care of 100 through 149. This matches 100 to 149 and lastly to match 150 we'll just match the characters themselves. Now let's put it all together and add anchors so we'll only match a number in this range and nothing else. This section matches zero through 99. The middle set matches 100 through 149, and the next set matches 150. Grouping and alternation combined with occurrence operands

> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/alternation-and-grouping-with-regexes?u=76281980&t=187)** can be very powerful in regular expressions. If you choose to try this in Grep, make sure you pass the dash capital E option so it processes it as an ERE. Also make sure you include the anchors or you may end up with a lot more matches than you think you should.

#### [Back references and subexpressions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/back-references-and-subexpressions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/back-references-and-subexpressions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I've mentioned before that in GNU tools, BREs and EREs are equal. It's interesting to note that in the POSIX standard, EREs do not support back references. If you're using POSIX tools you need to convert these to BREs to get them to work. A back reference is a pattern that's stored in a buffer to be recalled later. We can remember up to nine patters which is the limit. For instance if we wanted to match words that had more than one pair of S's, we'd first create our pattern of double S's and place it in parenthesis so it's remembered. And lastly we'd recall the pattern using a back reference. The format is backslash one, backslash two, and so on up to backslash nine. Note that this regular expression matches with double S's in the parenthesis and then matches again with the back reference. This regular expression would match double s's, followed by any number of characters and then more double s's. We can recall the pattern more than once as well. For instance, let's recall our double s pattern more than once. This would match classlessness, possessiveness, and stresslessness. You can remember more than one pattern to. For instance we can find five letter palindromes using two back references. Note that they're assigned from left to right, but we don't have to recall them in that order. This would match radar. In the case of radar, the first dot would match r and be stored in the numerically first back reference. The second dot would match A and would be stored in the second back reference. Then the third dot would match D, but since it's not in parenthesis it won't be remembered. Then we recall the matches using our second back reference first which holds the A, and lastly we recall the first back reference which holds the R matching radar.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/back-references-and-subexpressions?u=76281980&t=92)** This would also match other five letter palindromes such as civil, kayak, and level. Back references can be very powerful. Just keep in mind that if you're using non-GNU tools you may have to convert your regex to a BRE. Also keep in mind that you have a maximum of nine back references that you can use.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** gnu (2), posix (2), bre (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (3), keep in mind (2)

#### [Search text using regular expressions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/search-text-using-regular-expressions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/search-text-using-regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've already talked about how to find text in a file using grep. Let's talk more about using grep in extended regular expressions. Grep may be the most common tool in [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) to use regular expressions with. In fact, the name grep stands for global regular expression print. By default, grep uses basic regular expressions, or BREs, but it supports EREs if you pass the dash E option, or you use the the E-grep command. The E-grep command name has been deprecated, but it's kept around for historical reasons, as some tools still rely on that name existing. We're going to focus on using EREs with grep in this video. If you want to force grep to process a regular expression as a BRE, you can pass the dash G option. Perhaps the only way of using perl compatible regular expressions in Bash is to pass the dah capital P option to grep. This is experimental and not all features are supported. Normally, the expression is assumed based on the syntax of commands, options and arguments. If you want to specify the expression you can pass it specifically using the dash E option. This allows you to get around the command option parsing problems when you have a regular expression that starts with a hyphen. In some cases, Bash may try to parse the hyphen as the start of a command option. We can also place patterns in a file one per line and call the file by passing the dash F option followed by the file name. We can store multiple patterns in this file and will be tried. We can also combine this option with a pattern or patterns passed with a dash E option and it will process all of them. To force grep to only match [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) without specifying this in the RegEx we can pass the dash W option.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/search-text-using-regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=95)** This is similar to using a back slash W special expression in an ERE. If we wanted to force grep to only match entire lines we can pass the dash X option. This is the same as anchoring the line at both he beginning and the end with a carat and dollar sign. Another useful option is the dash O which only shows the text that is matched in stead of the entire line. Lastly, dash Q is quiet mode. This is useful for processing return codes to signal the existence of a search item. Grep will return a code of zero if the text is found and a non-zero value if it is not. Nothing else will display in the screen. I'm going to use grep to search through a text file of words. This file is included in the exercise files for this course in the chapter nine directory. Make sure you have the exercise files extracted and are in the chapter nine directory and then typ in ls to verify. We'll use a regular expression with a back reference to search for words that have two sets of double S's using the words.txt file. Type in clear then type in grep, space, dash, capital E for ERE, space, single quote, left parenthesis, ss, right parenthesis, that's our group, dot asterisk, backslash one, single quote, the backslash one is our back reference, space, words.txt, and then hit enter. This shows all lines that have more than one set of double S's. I won't spend a lot of time talking about how to use regular expressions in grep as the regular expressions are the same for all GNU tools, but I will mention the grep relies on greedy matches, and you'll need to be specific when matching.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/search-text-using-regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=189)** For instance, type in clear and then type in grep, space, dash, capital E, space, single quote, A, dot asterisk, A, single quote, space, words.txt. Then hit enter. This returns every single line that includes an A followed by zero or more characters and another A. These results may not be what we want. If you want a word that starts and ends with the letter A, you'll want to pass the dash W option to grep. Type in clear, then bring your line back, and a dash W and hit enter. This is more specific, but it still gives us more results than we want. If you only want lines that start with the letter A and end with the letter A, you have two choices. You can switch to the dash X option by typing in clear, bringing your line back, and changing the dash W to dash X. Or your second option is to use anchors. Type in clear the type in grep, space, dash, capital E, space, single quote, carat is the anchor for the beginning of the line, A, dot asterisk, A, dollar sign is the anchor for the end of the line, single quote, space, words.txt, and hit enter. I like using anchors because I can use this regular expression with other tools. Whereas the dash X is only useful for grep. Another useful option that's specific to grep is dash O. This only returns the pattern that is matched. This would similar to what ends up in the Bash_rematch array. Let's bring our original line back. Type in clear and then bring your original line back. This would be the one without a dash W or a dash X,
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/search-text-using-regular-expressions?u=76281980&t=285)** and then hit enter. We can see that the matched part is red. Now let's add the dash O to see the difference, and hit enter. You should only see the matched portion which was highlighted in the previous example. This is useful when using the backslash special expressions such as backslash B or backslash W, which matched boundaries but doesn't include them. Before we had regular expression matching in Bash we had to use grep. We can still do this by including it in an if conditional. In this example, we only relay on the return code of grep and not the output. Although this is still a valid method of searching a file, it might not be the most efficient way of using irregular expression if the data's already in your Bash script in a variable or array.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-regular-expressions-to-delete-change-and-substitute-text?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For this video, we're going delete, change and substitute texts using the sed stream editor and regular expressions. > **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-regular-expressions-to-delete-change-and-substitute-text?u=76281980&t=35)** The pattern that we're matching is the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "root", which is between the forward slashes and then printing the result, which is two lines. Type in "clear", then type in "sed -n 's", for substitute, "/[caret]root", that's what we're looking for "/toor/", which is what we're substituting with, "p' passwd" and then hit enter. Note that we're substituting n by confining the -n option with the p substitution option, we're only printing the line where modified. If you want to print all lines, remove the -n option, bring the line back, delete the -n and hit enter again. And you'll see the line that starts with root has been changed. Note this doesn't actually change the file, but outputs the changes to standard out. Now let's use some regular expressions. We use an extended regular expression for this. You can tell sed to use extended regular expressions by using the /r option or the /E option. The latter is POSIX compliant. Type in clear. Type in "sed /E" for extended regular expression, " 's" for substitute, "/" and then we'll start our pattern.

Remember that sed does a non-greedy match by default. We're going to replace it with the word "uid". Hit enter. You'll notice that the third column is now uid if the original number was less than 1,000. We can also delete patterns just by substituting them with nothing. For instance, if we left out the :uid: it would've removed the column completely. Now let me show you another neat thing that sed does. We have back references, but we can also duplicate the [Mesh](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Mesh.md) pattern. There's a file in chapter nine directory called "phonenumbers.txt". This is very simple with the n percent character. It recalls what was matched and just replays it. Type in "clear" then type in "sed -E 's", for substitute, "/". For the first group of numbers, type in "([0-9", that's a Range, "]". For the modifier, "{3}", and then to close it off ")". For the second group, let's do a "({0-9]", and for the modifier we'll do three again. So, "{3})". For the last group of numbers, do "([0-9]".
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-regular-expressions-to-delete-change-and-substitute-text?u=76281980&t=262)** For the modifier this time we'll use four. "{4}" and we'll finish up the group with a ")". This is the pattern we're going to match. Now let's replace it using back references. "/(\1)" that's the first back reference. Then, "\2" is the second back reference. "-\3" is the third back reference "/'". And we'll specify the same "phonenumbers.txt" file, and then hit enter. This remembers three patterns and we replay them with back references in the substitution portion of the sed line. The first back reference is inside parenthesis, but again that's just normal characters that we wanted in the output. You can use sed to search and replace text but also much much more. For more examples of what you can do with sed, check out the sed one liners page.

#### [An introduction to Vim](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/an-introduction-to-vim?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/an-introduction-to-vim?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Although Nano is easy to start and get around in, I find it limiting and unintuitive at times. Another editor that's installed by default on just about all versions of [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) is VI or in the case of SENTAS, VIM or VIM proved. A lot of people shy away from using VIM because it virtually has no user interface and looks hard. This is unfortunate because VIM is incredibly powerful and efficient. VIM is so focused on speed and efficiency that it has one letter short cuts for two letter commands. It's important to note that VIM has three modes, insert mode, where you can insert text in a document, command mode which allows you to send commands through the editor via shortcut keys and x mode which allows us to type commands on the bottom line. Lets get started by running VIM in a terminal. VIM space VIM file dot t-x-t. Hit enter. This will start VIM editing a file on our current directory called VIM file dot t-x-t. Don't worry if you don't have it yet, as we'll save the file from inside VIM. You'll notice that VIM has very little in the way of interface. VIM's user interface comprises of one line as a bottom of the screen. I recommend keeping and eye on this line as it's the only indication of what VIM is doing. When opening a file for the first time the bottom line says it's a new file. The bottom line will also show you how many lines the file has. Lets go into insert mode by pressing the i key. You may notice that the bottom line has now changed to insert. Lets add some text. Go to command mode by pressing in the escape key. You will notice the bottom line
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/an-introduction-to-vim?u=76281980&t=91)** is now blank again, this means we're in command mode. In command mode we interact with VIM through keyboard shortcuts. Move your cursor to the last line of text and press d d. We just deleted the last line of text. The third mode is x mode, which allows us to enter commands on the bottom line. To go into x mode type escape colon. Pressing escape takes us out of insert mode, typing the colon puts us in x mode. Notice the bottom line is now a colon. Lets type w and hit enter. This lets us write the changes to the disk. If we wanted to write to a different file, enter the name. Escape colon w space. And then lets type new file dot t-x-t and hit enter. To quit we can type in q. So escape colon q enter. Now that we're back on our command prompt, lets bring our line back using our up arrow key and hit enter to edit the file again. Lets go into insert mode by pressing i and we'll add another line of text. Now lets go to x mode and quit. Press escape colon q enter. We get this error message because we haven't yet saved the changes. We can either save first and then quit or we can force quit using the exclamation point. Lets force quit. Press escape colon q exclamation point and hit enter. This allowed us to quit without saving the changes. Lets bring our line back by pressing

They need to be in this order though as VIM processes them from left to right. Press escape colon w q and hit enter. This allowed us to write and then quit.

### 10. Working with Files

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [File globbing and parameter expansion](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/file-globbing-and-parameter-expansion?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/file-globbing-and-parameter-expansion?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the reasons that manipulating files on the command line can be faster and more efficient than the GUI is pattern matching. In Bash, pattern matching is called file globbing, and all commands can use it. File globs allow us to match patterns. For instance, if we wanted to list config files, we could match files ending in .conf by using an asterisk. So we type in ls space slash etc slash asterisk .conf. An asterisk matches any type of character and any number of them. So file.* would match any [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) that starts with file, has a dot in it, and is followed by any number of characters. A question mark matches any type of character but only one of them. So file?.txt would match filea.txt and file1.txt. If you want to match two characters, you're going to have to include two question marks. We can match a range or a list of characters with a set using square brackets. Each set matches exactly one character, but that one character can be any of the items in the range or list. By placing a hyphen between the start and ending characters, we can create a range. Without the hyphen, the characters are treated as a list. So file, left square bracket, zero through nine, right square bracket, .txt, would match file1.txt and file2.txt. Without the hyphen, it becomes a list, so the bottom example, file, left square bracket, abc123, right square bracket, dot jpg
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/file-globbing-and-parameter-expansion?u=76281980&t=92)** would match filea, fileb, and file3 and so on. If we want to match a hyphen, then we need to include the hyphen at the beginning or end of the list of characters in the set. We can also negate characters to match by placing an exclamation mark inside the set. For instance, to not match a number that's zero through nine, we'd use left square bracket, exclamation mark, zero through nine, right square bracket. [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) also provides us a mechanism for matching certain character classes. With character classes, we have a bit more power than the standard sets. To match numbers, you use left square bracket, colon, digit, colon, right square bracket. To match uppercase letters, we could use the upper character class. For lowercase characters, we could use a lower character class. If you want to match both upper and lower characters, use the alpha character class. To match uppercase, lowercase, and digits, use the alnum character class. The next one is a bit more difficult to do without a character class. To match whitespaces, such as spaces, tabs, and newlines, use the space character class. For printable characters not including spaces, use the graph character class, and for printable characters including spaces, use the print character class. To only match punctuation, use the punct character class. And for nonprintable characters, cntrl character class. And lastly, to match hexadecimal characters,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/file-globbing-and-parameter-expansion?u=76281980&t=187)** use the xdigit character class. The character class equivalent to left square bracket, zero through nine, right square bracket, is left square bracket, colon, digit, colon, right square bracket. It's longer to type but more reliable. Keep in mind that you need to place the character class inside additional square brackets of a set, so you'd actually type in left square bracket, left square bracket, colon, digit, colon, right square bracket, right square bracket. To match more than one character class, just include all desired character classes inside the set. For example, to match both digits and spaces, we can place both the digit and space character classes in the set. We can also negate a class. If we want to match everything but digits, place an exclamation mark between the outer square brackets of the set and the inner square brackets of the character class. Note, using the exclamation point inside the outer square brackets negates everything inside of them, including multiple character classes. Keep in mind that all sets, whether they're ranges, lists, or characters classes, match exactly one character. We're just determining which character can be in that position. If we want to match whole words instead of just letters, we can use brace expansion. For instance, type in ls space, left curly bracket, asterisk, .jpg, comma, asterisk .gif, comma, asterisk .png, right curly bracket.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/file-globbing-and-parameter-expansion?u=76281980&t=283)** This will match any word ending in .jpg, any that ends in .gif, or any that ends in .png.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **File Paths:** file1.txt (2), filea.txt (1), file2.txt (1)

A relative path never starts with a forward slash. If I type in CD, space, dot, dot, it'll take me to my parent directory in relation to where I was. My new path is relative to where I used to be. To demonstrate relative paths, let's change to user local, CD, space, slash, usr, slash, local, hit enter, and verify. I have a quick rule to decide if I should start the path with a slash or not. If I type in CD, space, etc, it will take me into user local etc. This is the relative path to etc. If I had typed in CD, space, slash, etc, and hit Enter, it would have worked, but I would have been in a completely different location. That would be the absolute path to slash etc. We will use the commands in this video more than any other in this course, so it's important to be comfortable with understanding absolute and relative paths.

#### [Copy files and dirs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/copy-files-and-dirs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/copy-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=0)** - Everyday file management requires us to copy files and directories. We can do these tasks from the GUI, but they're usually faster on the command line. The command we use to copy files on [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) is CP. The syntax for CP is CP [Options] <source> <destination> The options can take the form of single letters preceded by a hyphen, or complete words preceded by two hyphens. If they are single letters, they can be combined into one sequence. For instance,
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/copy-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=30)** - pf. The source file is the path to the file you want to make a copy of. If you want to copy multiple files, just separate them with spaces. You can also use file "globbing"- covered in this course- to match multiple files. The destination is where you want the file copied to. Both source and destination paths can either be relative paths, or absolute paths. Relative paths are relative to where you are. Absolute paths start with a forward slash and are from the root or top level directory. You can combine relative and absolute paths for source and destination files. However, there can only be one destination path. In my example, I use a tilde shortcut to indicate that I want to copy the file to my home directory. In a terminal, let's create a directory to create files in using "mkdir" and change into it. Type in "mkdir copyexercise", and hit "enter". Now, "cd" into "copyexercise", and hit "enter" again. You can verify your path by using the print working directory command. Type in "pwd" to make sure that you're in the "copyexercise" directory. Now let's create some files to copy using "touch". Touch's job is to create timestamps. But if the file we want to change doesn't exist, it creates an empty file, and that's all we need for this exercise. Type in "touch file1.txt" and hit "enter". Now let's make a copy of this file. Type in "cp file1.txt file1-copy.txt"

> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/copy-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=128)** and hit "enter". You won't see output on the screen if it was successful. >
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/copy-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=224)** and hit "enter". And then we'll verify again with Tree. Now we can see that inside "dir1", we have a "file1-copy.txt" as well. If you run the exact same line again, it will overwrite the destination file without a warning. Use your "Up-Arrow" key to get back to your copy line, and hit "enter". If you want Linux to warn us, we can add the "-I" option for interactivity. Use your "up-arrow" key again move over to CP, and insert a "-I". So your line should say "cp -I file1.txt dir1/file1-copy.txt".

> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/copy-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=269)** Now hit "enter". When it comes to copying a directory, things are different. Let's make a copy of our "dir1" directory and call it "dir2". Type in "cp dir1 dir2" and hit "enter". We get an error message saying that "cp" is omitting "dir1". This is not a very helpful message. What it's saying is that we didn't tell "cp" to enter the directory and copy the contents. To do so, we need to add the recursive option. Type in "cp -R dir1 dir2" and hit "enter" and verify with the Tree command. Type in "Tree" and hit "enter" again. You'll see that we now have a copy of "dir1" named "dir2". I'm not going to cover every option of the "Cp" command, but a couple of options I've found useful are: "-a" for Archive, which preserves all [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md). This includes timestamps, ownership, and permissions. "-b" to backup destination files, "-n" to not overwrite destination files if they exist, and "-u" to only copy files if they're newer than the destination. For more options, check Demand Page to CP by typing in "man cp".

#### [Move and rename files and dirs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/move-and-rename-files-and-dirs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/move-and-rename-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) copies a file, it copies the file data blocks to a new location on the disk. When moving a file between file systems, Linux copies the data blocks to the new location and then deletes the original data blocks. If however, the source and destination are on the same file system, Linux treats the file differently. If we have a file at /home/bob/file1.txt and we want to move it to /home/ted, we'd use the move command, or mv command. Since both of these locations are on the same hard drive, Linux just updates the file's location in the file system. This is very fast because no data is actually being moved on the disk. We can also move a file from a directory to the same directory, as long as we specify a different name. If we move /home/ted/file1.txt to /home/ted/file2.txt, it effectively renames it. This is why we use the mv command to rename files in Linux. The syntax for move is mv, which is the command name, and then the options. The options can take the form of single letters preceded by a hyphen, or complete words preceded by two hyphens. If they are single letters, they can be combined into one sequence. For instance, -bf. The source is the path to the file you want to move. If you want to move multiple files, just separate them with spaces.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/move-and-rename-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=94)** You can also use file globbing, covered in this course, to match file names. The destination is where you want the file moved to. Both the source and destination file paths can either be relative paths, or absolute paths, however, there can only be one destination path. In my example, I've used a tilde shortcut to indicate that I want to move the file to my home directory. In a terminal, let's make a directory to create files in using the mkdir command, and then change into it. Type in mkdir, space, moveexercise, and hit enter.
