#### [Remove files and dirs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/remove-files-and-dirs?u=76281980)

Type in your password and hit Enter again. Once it's installed, I'm going to clear my screen, and then I'll type in tree, and we can see that we'll have a visual representation of the file system. Let's start out by removing filea.txt, using the rm command. Type in rm filea.txt, and hit Enter. If it doesn't prompt us, then interactivity is turned off. To turn it on you can use a -i flag. So, rm -i fileb.txt, will delete fileb using rm with interactivity turned on. Hit Enter, and you'll notice that it prompts you to delete the file. Say y for yes and hit Enter. You can type in ls to verify that the files are gone. To delete a directory, we can use rmdir. Let's type in rmdir dir2, and hit Enter. Type in ls to verify that it's gone. And now let's delete dir1, type in rmdir dir1 and hit Enter. And we get an error message. We get an error message when using rmdir on dir1, and the reason for this is that rmdir only deletes empty directories. I recommend always trying to delete a directory with rmdir first, if it's not empty, Linux will warn you. So, to delete a directory with files in it,
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/remove-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=176)** you have to use the recursive option to rm. Let's also turn on interactivity, so we can see what it's doing. Type in rm -Ri. So, -R is for recursive, and i is for interactive. rm -Ri dir1. So, your line should say rm -Ri dir1, and hit Enter. It's asking us to descend into dir1. Say yes. Now it's asking to remove file1, say yes. And now it's asking to remove dir1, say yes again. Type in ls and you'll see that the directory is now gone. We have two files remaining. We can delete these two files using an asterisk wildcard, by typing rm *, but don't hit Enter yet. I really don't recommend doing this, especially with interactivity turned off, as it deletes all files in the current directory. If you accidentally did this in /, it would delete your entire operating system. I recommend making your pattern matching more strict. So, let's backspace that for a moment. The two files we have left are filec.txt and filed.txt. So, let's talk about file globbing for a moment. We have a couple of options. For a pattern we could use file[cd].txt, and this will match both filec and filed. Another option would be brace expansion, and your pattern would be file{c,d}.txt. That also will only match filec and filed .txt.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/remove-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=273)** Another not so strict option would be file?.txt, which also would match filec and filed, but also would have matched filea and fileb as well, so be careful. The last option uses an extended glob, which will need to be turned on. You'll probably want to view the video on extended globs in this course. The pattern would be file+, which means one or more, (c|d).txt, this means one or more c's or d's. Now, this would also match file ccc.txt as well, so it's not as strict as the first two. In this instance, I would recommend using the square brackets, or the brace expansion, as they're the most strict. Keep in mind that you don't have to try these file globs with a destructive command like rm. They're a function of the shell, so you can use them with any command. I recommend ls. So as an example, let's type in ls file[cd].txt, and hit Enter. You can see that we matched both of those files. Now that we know that we've matched only the files that we wanted, we can change this over to rm. Bring your command back by using the up arrow, and then just replace ls with rm. And we can verify by typing in ls. rm doesn't have a lot of options, and rmdir has even fewer. But you can check out the man pages for them to know more. For the man page for rm, just type in man rm.

#### [Create links to files and dirs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-links-to-files-and-dirs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-links-to-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] When organizing our workspace, it can be advantageous to create links or shortcuts to our files and directories. For instance, we could have a link in our home directory called Netdrive that points to a network drive that's at "/media/network/server/bob/files". This saves time browsing to it as it's a shortcut in our home directory. There are two different kinds of links: Symbolic Links and Hard Links. We'll start out by creating Hard Links. In a terminal, let's create a directory for this exercise. Type in "mkdir" space "lnexercise" and hit enter. Then "cd" and "lnexercise" and hit enter again. You can verify your paths by typing in "pwd". Now let's create a file in a directory. Type in "touch" space "file.txt" and hit enter. And then create a directory by typing in "mkdir" space "dir1". We can verify this with "ls" space "-l". We can see that we have a file named "file.txt" and a directory named "dir1". Now let's create a Hard Link to "file.txt" and we'll name that Hard Link "filelink.txt". The syntax is "ln" space "file.txt" space "filelink.txt" and hit enter. Let's verify this with "ls" space "-l" and hit enter. We'll see that we have a "file.txt" and a "filelink.txt". You may also notice that all file attributes are exactly the same between these two files including the time stamp. The reason for this is that "file.txt" and "filelink.txt" are exactly the same file.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-links-to-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=96)** When we create a Hard Link it just gives the file system blocks another file name pointing to them. In this case, "file.txt" and "filename.txt" point to the exact same data blocks. Hard Links take virtually no space on the desk and are completely transparent to the OS in applications. So transparent in fact, that they're hard to identify. In order to free data blocks to a file, you have to remove all inodes pointing to it. In the case of Hard Links, you have to remove all Hard Links before the data blocks are freed. You my also notice that both "file.txt" and "filelink.txt" have two inodes in the second column. Normally files have one inode so this is your hint that it is a Hard Link. If you look up above, you'll see that "file.txt" used to have one inode. We can verify this with a stat command. Type in "stat" space "file.txt" and hit enter. We can see that it says it has two links, and the inode number is "8619397". Now let's use the stat command on "filelink.txt". Type in "stat" space "filelink.txt" and hit enter. We see that this also has two links, and that the inode is exactly the same. There are a couple of limitations to Hard Links. Hard Links cannot link to directories. Hard Links cannot link across filesystems meaning to files on another partition. Hard Links are difficult to identify due to looking identical to their target file. The positives to Hard Links are:
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-links-to-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=189)** They take up virtually no space, and they don't break when the target is deleted. The second type of link is a Symbolic Link. I'm going to clear my screen and then we'll create a Symbolic Link. A Symbolic Link is a file that points to another file. Let's create a Symbolic Link that points to "fileone.txt" and we'll use "ln" again, but we'll use a "-s" option. So type in "ln" space "-s" space "file.txt" space "filesymlink.txt" and hit enter. Now let's verify this with a long list. Type in "ls" space "-l" and you can see that we have a new file here that looks very different than a hard link. The left-most character is an "l" meaning it's a Symbolic Link. We can also see in the right-hand column what the link is pointing to. You may also notice that the permissions are drastically different than the original file. The permissions are "rwxrwxrwx". This just means that when you access the link, it passes you onto the real file, and the permissions on the real file are enforced. Now let's delete the original file and see what happens. Type in "rm" space "file.txt" and hit enter, and then verify with a long list. Notice that the Symbolic Link is broken and turned red, but the Hard Link still looks fine. You may also notice that the inode number on the Hard Link has gone to one. There are a couple of negatives to Symbolic Links. They take up a small amount of space, and they break if the target is deleted.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-links-to-files-and-dirs?u=76281980&t=283)** There are a few positive attributes to Symbolic Links though. Including: They can link across filesystems, they can link to directories, and they're easy to identify using "ls -l".

#### [Manage user passwords](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-user-passwords?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-user-passwords?u=76281980&t=0)** - The main tool for managing passwords in [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) is the "passwd" command. If you type in "passwd" without any arguments, it asks us twice for a new password and then the password is saved. The user account information is saved in the /etc/passwd file. It may seem like a file with this name would hold a password, and it did in the past. However, those files were readable, so even though the password was encoded, people could still hack away at it as long as they wanted. Linux then started storing the password in the /etc/shadow file, along with password aging information. This file is only readable by root. Administrators can use the password tool to lock and unlock passwords, as well as set some account aging information. In this video, we will not focus on account aging. See the man page for the "chage" command for more information. Let's cover some of the "passwd" command's more common options. "-d" deletes a user's password. This is basically the state of a newly created user without a password. "-e" expires a password. A user with an expired password will need to reset it at the next login. "-l" locks the password; "-u" unlocks the password. Note that using "passwd -l" only locks the password, not the account. So if a user had a valid SSH key, they could log in anyway. To lock the account, you'll need to use the "chage" command with the expire option and the value set to zero. To unlock the account, we could use the "chage" command again, and change the expire to "-1". This sets the account to never expire. We could also enter a date so the account would expire on a certain day.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-user-passwords?u=76281980&t=93)** "-S" outputs the password status, and lastly, to view password account aging information, use "chage -l". In a terminal, let's create a new user named user1 and give it a password. Once it has a valid account, we'll lock the password with the "passwd" command. Type in "sudo" + Space + useradd" + Space + "user1" and hit Enter. Type in your password when prompted. Let's verify that user1 does not have a password yet by looking at /etc/shadow file. Type in "sudo" + Space + "cat" + Space + "/etc/shadow" and hit Enter. We can see in the second column, there are two exclamation points. This means they do not have a password. Now let's give the user a password. Type in "clear", then type in "sudo" + Space + "passwd" + Space + "user1" and hit Enter. Now enter their password twice. We'll verify that user1 has a password by looking at the /etc/shadow file again. And now in the second column, there's a long encoded password. Remember this file can only be read by root, so even though the password is encoded, regular users can't even see it to try to hack it. Now let's lock the password and look at /etc/shadow again. Type in "sudo" + Space + "passwd" + Space + "-l" + Space + "user1" and hit Enter. Now let's look at the /etc/shadow file. Type in "sudo" + Space + "cat" + Space + "/etc/shadow" and hit Enter again. Now the password is preceded by double exclamation marks. "passwd -l" only locks the password, and as such, a user could log in with SSH keys. To fully lock a user account, use "chage".
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-user-passwords?u=76281980&t=188)** Type in "clear" then type in "sudo" + Space + "chage" + Space + "-E" + Space + "0" + Space + "user1" and hit Enter. To verify, type in "sudo" + Space + "chage" + Space + "-l" Space "user1" and hit Enter again. Notice that user1's account expired on January 1st, 1970. To unlock the account, bring your line back and change "0" to "-1" and hit Enter. Now let's verify again with "chage". Type in "sudo" + Space + "chage" + Space + "-l" + Space + "user1" and hit Enter. Now you can see that user1's account never expires. To see the status of a user's password, we'll use the "passwd" command again with a "-S" option. Type in "clear" and then type in "sudo" + Space + "passwd" + Space + "-S" + Space + "user1" and hit Enter. This shows that the user1's password is still locked, even though we unlocked the user account using the "chage" command. The password, which is different, is still locked. To unlock it, use "passwd" again. Type in "sudo" + Space + "passwd" + Space + "-u" + Space + "user1" and hit Enter. And then we'll verify again.

#### [What is a file?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-is-a-file?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-is-a-file?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In most operating systems, a file is an object such as a document or photo that's saved on a hard disk. These files are organized usin' named folders in a hierarchy. The first type of file that we'll talk about is a directory. A directory is a list of other files. It helps us organize our system so it's easier to find our files. We can identify directories by looking at the first character on the left of a long list using ls base -l. That first character should be the letter d. If the first charter's a hyphen, then it's a regular file. This includes most traditional files like documents and photos. If the first character's an l then it's a symbolic link. You can think of a symbolic link as a shortcut to another file. Viewing these files with ls base -l will show you where the link is pointing to. If the first character's a c, it's a character device file. These files output or input characters. For instance, the /dev/random device file outputs random characters. If the first character's a b, then it's a block device file. This file represents block devices such as hard drives or USB thumb drives. A socket file has an s as the first character. A socket file facilitates the communication of one application to another. If the first character's a p then the file's a named pipe. A named pipe is also called a FIFO or first-in, first-out. These are buffers for placin' data in a queue to be processed by other applications. [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) takes the idea of everything being a file
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-is-a-file?u=76281980&t=94)** so far that even the output of application data on the screen is considered a file that hasn't yet been saved. The /proc directory has files with data in them that can be read but have a file size of zero bytes. These are attributes and information about the Linux OS presented in a form of regular files and directories. They don't physically exist, but appear and act just like normal files and are created on the fly by the kernel when they're read. This idea of everything being a file allows us to view important system information using nothing but simple file viewers. It's a very powerful concept.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) (2)
> **Analogies:** such as (2), for instance (1), just like (1)
> **CLI Commands:** ls (2), find (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **Env Vars:** usb (1), fifo (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Get file attributes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-file-attributes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-file-attributes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Data that describes other data is called [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md). It's important to be able to view metadata describing attributes of our files. These attributes may include the file name, its size, permissions and ownership, and access time. The quickest way to get information about a file is by doing a long list. In a terminal, type in ls space dash l space slash etc slash passwd and hit enter. This will give us a long list of the Etsy password file. Let's take a look. In the first column is the file type. In our example, the Etsy password file has a hyphen, so it's a regular file. The next three characters is the user owner's permissions. The possible options are r for read, w for write, and x for execute. The user owner is the third column from the left. In our case, the user owner is root. The next three characters are the group owner's permissions. Every file is owned by one user owner and one group owner. The group owner is the fourth column from the left. The group owner of this file is the root group, and its members can only read this file. The last group of three characters in the first column are permissions for other. Other contains anyone who's not the user owner or the group owner. Other in this example has read-only access. The next character is the number of Inodes, or index nodes, that the file uses.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-file-attributes?u=76281980&t=93)** An Inode stores the metadata about the file. Most regular files only have one Inode. A directory will have one Inode for itself, one for its parent, and one for each of the files inside of it. The third column is the user owner of the file. A file can only belong to one user owner. The fourth column is the group owner. A file can only belong to one group owner. The fifth column is the size of the file in bytes. By providing ls, the dash h option, you could see the output in kilobytes, megabytes, or gigabytes. The ninth column is the name of the file. In a terminal, let's view hidden files by typing in ls space dash la and hit enter. Any file or directory name that starts with a dot is hidden. We can only see hidden files by adding the dash a option to ls. The output of ls shows us permissions, user and group ownership, and the size of the file. To see what kind of file type it is, we'll use the file command. Type in file space slash etc slash passwd and hit enter. We can see that the Etsy password file is ASCII text. We have another command that can show us metadata about a file. Type in stat space slash etc slash passwd and hit enter. Stat shows us the name, size, Inodes, permissions,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-file-attributes?u=76281980&t=192)** ownership, and SELinux security context. It also has the last access data just like the ls dash l command. The next two lines below last access is modify and change. Modify is the last time the file contents have been modified. Change is the last time the file metadata has been updated. The metadata would update, for example, if we changed ownership or permissions.

#### [Get extended attributes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-extended-attributes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-extended-attributes?u=76281980&t=0)** Extended attributes are extensions to normal file attributes. Just like normal attributes, extended attributes are stored with files on the disk. There are three major types of extended attributes. Extended security attributes, extended system attributes, and extended user attributes. Let's take a closer look at each. Each file has a SCLinux security context as stored with the file. To get the SCLinux security context, use ls with a -Z option. Have an ls -Z /etc/passwd and hit enter.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-extended-attributes?u=76281980&t=38)** We can see the SCLinux security context in the fifth column. We can also recursively get the SCLinux security context using the -RZ option. Extended system attributes store file access control list, or ACLs. ACLS provide an additional layer of discretionary access control for [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md), making Linux permissions easier to manage. File ACLs allow administrators to set permissions for multiple users on a file. They also allow permissions for multiple groups to be set up on the file. ACLs provide an inheritance for user and group permissions, so newly created files can inherit permissions from the parent directory. ACLs also provide the ability to back up and restore permissions easily. We haven't created a file with an ACL yet, so let's do that now using touch. Type in touch aclfile.txt and hit enter. Let's take a look at the permissions on this file using LS, type in ls -l aclfile.txt. We can see in my case my user name Grant has rewrite permissions. And my group, named Grant, also has rewrite permissions. And other has read only. Now let's set an ACL in the file using the setfacl command. Type in setfacl -m user:root:rwx aclfile.txt and hit enter.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-extended-attributes?u=76281980&t=135)** Now that we have a file with an ACL, we can list it using the getfacl command. Type in getfacl -t aclfile.txt and hit enter.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-extended-attributes?u=76281980&t=149)** The getfacl command stores standard Linux permissions and ownership, it also shows the additional ACL for the root user that we just set. We can recursively get ACLs using the -R option. Later in the course, we will cover ACLs in detail. Extended user attributes store additional flags and files aptly named extended attributes. Files can be set append only, so we can add data to them, but not overwrite the original. Files can have the compress attribute set so they are compressed and decompressed automatically. The immutable flag on a file means it can not be modified. They can not be deleted or renamed, and no data can be written to the file. When the backup flag is set, and a file is deleted, the data's backed up so we can undelete it later. Normally this is not the case with Linux. In a terminal let's create a file and set an attribute. Type in touch afile.txt. We'll need to elevate privileges to set the attribute. Type in sudo chattr +i afile.txt and hit enter.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/get-extended-attributes?u=76281980&t=216)** Type in your password and hit enter again. Now that we have a file with an extended attribute, we can retrieve it using the lsattr command. Type in lsattr afile.txt and hit enter. Viewing the extended attributes with lsattr shows the immutable flag that we just set. We can recursively get extended attributes using the -R option. You can get more information on extended attributes by viewing the man page for change attribute. All of these extended attributes add layers of security to a Linux system in different ways.

#### [About standard Linux permissions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-standard-linux-permissions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-standard-linux-permissions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The standard [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) permission system came from Unix and was created 40 years ago. It is a tried and true system and works for most situations. The Linux permission system supports the following items. Users can belong to multiple groups. Groups cannot, however, contain other groups. Files and directories belong to one user owner. Files and directories belong to one group owner. Permissions can be set for the user group or other, other being people who are not the user owner and don't belong to the group owner. Users can read, write, or execute files. Users can list items in directories, create new files in directories, and traverse directories. Linux supports privilege escalation to the user owner or group owner of the file. Linux supports group owner inheritance. This means that files and directories can inherit the parent directory's group owner. Linux supports default file permissions that can be different for each user. Linux permissions aren't perfect, though. Here are there shortcomings. Later in this course, we'll talk about access control lists, which will remedy these issues. Files and directories can only belong to one user owner. Files and directories can only belong to one group. These two become an issue if you want to assign permissions to more than one user or group. Permissions set to other are not concise. We don't know who other is, we just know that they're not the user owner or the group owner. Inheritance only supports group ownership and not permissions. A child file or directory can only inherit the parent directory's group owner,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-standard-linux-permissions?u=76281980&t=93)** not permissions. There's no easy way to backup or restore permissions. There's also no easy way to temporarily restrict permissions. Even with these limitations, the Linux permissions system handles most situations well.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [File and directory modes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/file-and-directory-modes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/file-and-directory-modes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Standard [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) permissions support three different modes, read, write and execute. These three modes provide different functionality for files and directories. For files, read access means a user can open and read the contents of a file. When a user has write access to a file they can write or modify the contents. When a user has execute permissions on a file it means that the file can be run as an application. Commands like LS and applications like Firefox would have their execute bit set. What happens when a command is executed is it's loaded into memory, and run until told to stop. These same three modes act differently on a directory. If a user has read access to a directory, it means they can list the contents of the directory, which includes the [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md) about the files and directories in it. If a user doesn't have read access and they type in LS inside the directory, you'll see a lot of question marks where the metadata should be. If a user has write access to a directory that allows them to write to the directory. Writing to the directory means creating new files in it. Execute permissions on a directory are a bit odd. You're not going to run a directory like you would a command. Execute permissions means that you can enter or traverse the directory.

>

> **Definitions:** means that (2)

#### [Set permissions using numeric method](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-numeric-method?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-numeric-method?u=76281980&t=0)** There are two different methods of setting permissions in [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md): numeric and symbolic. Let's talk about numeric mode now. We have three positions in Linux permissions: User Owner, Group Owner, and other. We can set read, write, and execute permissions on files and directories. In numeric mode, we assign a value to each permission and add the numbers to get the final result. Read access has a value of four, write access has a value of two, and execute is worth one. To set read, write, and execute for the User Owner, we'd add up four plus two plus one, for a total of seven. To set read and execute for the Group Owner, we'd add up four and one, for a total of five. And for other, we don't give any permissions, so we'll set it to zero. Now we'll use this number in all three positions, User Owner, Group Owner, and other. The command we use to set permissions is chmod, or change mode. The syntax for chmod is chmod, space, options, space, permissions, space, file name. Let's go to a terminal and create a directory for this exercise. In a terminal, type, mkdir space permexercise and hit enter. Now cd into permexercise and hit enter again. We can verify our path by typing in pwd. Now let's create a file using touch.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-numeric-method?u=76281980&t=95)** Type in touch, space, file.txt, and hit enter. Now let's verify this by typing in ls space, dash l. We can see that our file already has permissions. The User Owner permissions are read-write, the Group Owner permissions are read-write, and other is set to read. Default permissions are assigned to new files based on the value of umask. In another video of this course, we'll cover using umask. Let's use the chmod command to give the User Owner read, write, and execute, the Group Owner read and execute, and other will get no permissions. Read, write, and execute adds up to seven, read and execute adds up to five, and no permissions for other equates to zero. So we'll type in chmod, space, seven, five, zero, space, file.txt, and hit enter. Now let's verify this ls space dash l, and hit enter. We can see that the User Owner permissions are now read, write, execute, the Group Owner permissions are now read execute, and other has no permissions. We can also set permissions recursively by specifying the dash capital R option. However, I can't think of a practical application for using chmod recursively with numeric mode. There is a use case for recursive in symbolic mode, which we'll cover in another video. If you want more information about chmod,

#### [Set permissions using symbolic method](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-symbolic-method?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-symbolic-method?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are two different methods of setting permissions in [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md); numeric and symbolic mode. Let's talk about symbolic mode. We have three positions in Linux permissions; user owner, group owner, and other. We can set read, write, and execute permissions on files and directories. In symbolic mode, we assign a value to each position using symbolic representation of the desired permissions. To set read, write, and execute for the user owner, we just specify u equals rwx. To set read and execute for the group owner, we'd use g equals rx. To set permission to nothing for other, just do o equals with nothing after it. To set permission for multiple positions at one time, just separate them with a comma. For instance, u equals rwx comma g equals rx comma o equals.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-symbolic-method?u=76281980&t=63)** With symbolic method, we can also add permissions by changing the equal sign to a plus. To add rwx for the user, we would use u plus rwx. To subtract permissions, replace the equal sign with a hyphen. To take execute permissions away from other, we do o minus x. The command we use to set permissions is chmod, or change mode. The syntax for chmod is chmod space options space permissions space filename. Let's go to a terminal and create a directory for this exercise. In the terminal, type in mkdir space permsymexercise and hit Enter. Now, let's cd into our new directory, so we'll type in cd space permsymexercise and hit Enter. We can verify our path by typing in pwd. Now, let's create a file using touch. Type in touch space file.txt and hit Enter. Let's verify this with ls space dash l and hit Enter. We can see that the user owner of the file has read/write permissions. The group owner of the file has read/write permission as well and other has read-only. Default permission are assigned to new files based on the value of umask. In another video of this course, we over using umask. To change permissions, we'll use the chmod command. I'm going to give the user owner read, write,
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-symbolic-method?u=76281980&t=157)** and execute, the group owner, read and execute, and other will get no permissions. Type in chmod space u equals rwx comma g equals rx
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-symbolic-method?u=76281980&t=172)** comma o equals space file dot txt and hit Enter. Verify this with ls space dash l and hit Enter again. Now, we can see the user owner has read, write, execute permissions, the group owner has read and execute, and other has no permissions. One of the major advantages to symbolic method is that you can add or subtract permissions. The reason this is useful is that we don't have to know the current permission to add or take away. With numeric method, we'd had to get the current permissions and then, recalculate the desired permissions. Let's take execute permissions away from the group by typing in chmod space g minus x space file dot txt and hit Enter. Now, let's verify this by typing in ls space dash l and hit Enter. We can see that execute permissions were removed from the group. You can also group the positions. For instance, if you want to turn off execute for user group and other, we could do chmod space ugo minus x space file dot txt and hit Enter. We could also specify a for all positions, which would include user group and other. For instance, chmod space a minus x space file dot txt would also remove execute from all positions. We can also set permission recursively by specifying the dash capital R option. This is useful with symbolic mode
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-permissions-using-symbolic-method?u=76281980&t=267)** because we can add or subtract permissions. To set this up, let's create a username, bob. Type in sudo space user add space bob and hit Enter. Type in your password and hit Enter again. If we wanted to remove all execute permissions for every file in bob's home directory, we'd type in sudo space chmod space dash capital R space a minus x space slash home slash bob, which is bob's home directory, and hit Enter. Note, we have to elevate privileges using sudo because we're changing another user's permissions. For more information about chmod, check out the man page by typing in man space chmod and hit Enter.

#### [Default permissions using umask](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/default-permissions-using-umask?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/default-permissions-using-umask?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When files are created, default permissions are applied automatically. These permissions are calculated based on a bitmask called umask. To see a umask type into a terminal umask and hit enter. We can also view the umask in symbolic notation by using the -S option. Type in umask -S and hit enter. We can see that my umask is 0002, which equates to rwx for the user, rwx for the group and rx for other. Centos has different umasks for root and regular users. That's su the root and check the umask again. Type in su - root and hit enter. Type in root's password and hit enter again. Type in umask and hit enter again. You can see that root's umask is 0022, and my user's umask is 0002. Type in exit to go back to your user. Notice that the umask isn't the same format as numeric permissions such as 754, but is rather upside down. To calculate default permissions, we'll have to subtract the umask from maximum initial permissions. For directories, our maximum initial permissions are 777, since having execute on a directory doesn't create a [Security Risk](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Security%20Risk.md). Now let's subtract 002 from 777. The result is 775,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/default-permissions-using-umask?u=76281980&t=92)** or equivalent to rwx for the user owner, rwx for the group owner and rx for other. Now let's go to a terminal and check this out. Let's make a directory by typing in mkdir umaskdir and hit enter. Verify this with ls -l. We can see usmakdir. The default permissions are rwx, rwx and rx, or 775 just as our calculations would show. The maximum initial permissions for a file are different than for a directory. For security reasons, we don't allow execute permissions on files by default, so the maximum initial permissions on a file is 666 or rw for user, group and other. Having execute off by default keeps files from being automatically executable after being copied from one place to another. This includes files downloaded from the internet. To get the default permissions for files, subtract the right three digits of the umask from 666. My umask is 0002, so I will subtract 002 from 666, which results in rw for our user, rw for our group and read only for other. Let's check this out by going to the terminal and creating a file. Type in touch umaskfile.txt and hit enter. Verify this with ls -l and hit enter again. Note that the permissions on our new file
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/default-permissions-using-umask?u=76281980&t=188)** are at read/write for the user owner, read/write for the group owner, and read only for other, or 664 just as our calculation would show. We can temporarily change our umask by using the umask command. Type in umask 022 and hit enter. For directories a umask of 022 would give read/write execute for the user owner, read execute for the group owner and read execute for other. For a files it would give read/write for the user owner, read for the group owner and read for other. Verify the umask by typing in umask and hit enter. This only works for our current login session. If a user wants to change their umask, they can add it to their Bash startup file using an editor. We're going to use VI for this. Type in vi ~/.bashrc and hit enter.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/default-permissions-using-umask?u=76281980&t=241)** Go into insert mode by pressing the insert key, and we'll go to the very last line and hit enter. We'll add umask 0022 and we'll hit enter. Let's save this by pressing escape :wq and we'll hit enter again. Note that the change we just made is only for our current user. If an administrator wants to change a system-wide umask, they can add a file to /etc/profile.d. Let's create a new file there named umask.sh using VI. We'll need to elevate privileges with sudo. Type in sudo vi /etc/profile.d/umask.sh and hit enter.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/default-permissions-using-umask?u=76281980&t=289)** Type in your password and hit enter again. We could just add a line here that says umask 022, but it's customary to have a different unmask for root and regular users so we'll add a condition. Go into insert mode by pressing the insert key, and then type in if [ "$UID" -ge 1000 ] :then hit enter
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/default-permissions-using-umask?u=76281980&t=325)** tab umask 022 hit enter fi. What this will do is check the user ID of the currently logged in user, and if it's greater than or equal to 1000 it'll change the umask to 022. This won't take effect until you log in again. Let's save this by pressing escape :wq and hitting enter.

#### [Special file bits: SUID and SGID](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/special-file-bits-suid-and-sgid?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/special-file-bits-suid-and-sgid?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] SUID and SGID are special bits for bits for privilege escalation on executable files. The SUID bit allows non user owners to execute commands with the privileges of the user owner. In a terminal let's do a long list on slash usr, slash bin, slash su, by typing in ls, space, dash el, space, slash usr, slash bin, slash su, and hit enter. The first thing you may notice is that the file is bright red. This tells us something special is happening here. Let's take a look at the permissions. The permissions for the user owner are rws. Note that we can no longer see the user owners execute position. The s and the user owner's execute position means we have the SUID bit set. If the s is lower case then the execute is also set. If the s is upper case then the execute permissions are not set. The case of the s is the only way to tell if execute permissions are set or not. When the SUID bit is set and regular users such as Bob executes it their privileges get elevated to that of the user owner's. In our example Bob would be executing the su command as root. This means his command would have the power of the root user. To set the SUID bit we utilize an extra column of numeric permissions. First we decide what underlying permissions we want on the file. In this case the file has rwx, rx, rx,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/special-file-bits-suid-and-sgid?u=76281980&t=95)** or seven, five, five permissions. We then decide what extra bits we want. In this case SUID, which would equal to four. Set in these permissions via numeric mode would be chmod, space, 4755, space, slash usr, slash bin, slash su. We could also use symbolic method as well by typing in chmod, space u plus s, space, slash usr, slash bin, slash su. The SGID bit is very similar to SUID. When we have SGID set the s resides in the execute position of the group owner's permissions. When a regular user executes a command with SGID bit set it tuns with the privileges of the group owner of the file. In this case the screen group. To add SGID bits to a file we prefixed the permissions with two. For the screen command the permissions would be, 2, 7, 5, 5. We'd set SJD with chmod, space 2755, space, slash usr, slash bin, slash screen. We could also set this with symbolic mode in which we'd use chmod space g plus s, space, slash usr, slash bin, slash screen. Because these commands allow privilege escalation without prompting for a password, it maybe be advantageous to know where in the file system they are for security reasons. We can use the find command to locate SUID and SGID files.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/special-file-bits-suid-and-sgid?u=76281980&t=191)** In a terminal type sudo, space find, space slash, space dash perm, space dash 4000 and hit enter. Type in your password and hit enter again. Because we're searching for permissions that are greater than 4000, this will find all of the files with the SUID bit set. To find files with a SGID bit set we'll change the line slightly. Type in sudo space find, space slash, space dash perm, space dash 2000 for SGID and hit enter.

The t in the execute position of the other permissions is our sticky bit. It will be a lower case t if execute for other is set, otherwise it will be an uppercase T.

And we can see that even though we have read, write, execute permissions for user group and other, Bob cannot delete that file. The sticky bit is used mainly for world writeable

#### [Hard drive partitioning strategies](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/hard-drive-partitioning-strategies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/hard-drive-partitioning-strategies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've already chosen the automatic partitioning scheme during install. However, if you're installing a server from scratch and have special plans for that server, it may be good to create partitions manually. This gives you a lot of flexibility that you won't have with the automatic partitioning scheme. With other operating systems, the entire OS goes in one partition. With [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md), we have the ability to put pieces of it on many different discs just by mounting those volumes into the file system tree. For instance, we could segment off /boot, /home, and /var into separate partitions if we wanted to. The Linux mounting system is completely flexible. We can even mount a drive onto a directory deep within the file system such as /home/grant/data and have it be completely transparent to the user. When the user goes into the directory, they will not know it's a separate drive. In modern Linux, we have several different methods of dividing up a disc: BIOS partitions, GUID partitions, and logical volume management. We'll look at each of these in detail later in the course. BIOS partitions are divisions recognized by the BIOS. We are limited to exactly four real BIOS partitions. These partitions are called primary partitions. This limit is an addressing limitation in the BIOS. After this limit proved to be insufficient, a hack evolved. This hack allowed taking one of the four real partitions and labeling it as an extended partition. The purpose of the extended partition was to hold logical drives or software partitions. Logical drives had some limitations such as operating systems not being able to boot from them.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/hard-drive-partitioning-strategies?u=76281980&t=93)** As such, BIOS systems have to have one primary partition to boot from. When the BIOS was replaced by UEFI, a new type of partition table emerged: GUID partition table, or GBT. With a GUID partition table, the four disc limitation was lifted. As such, there's no need for extended partitions nor logical drives. Keep in mind that if you create a drive with a GUID partition table, older machines may not recognize those drives. This is, however, the only limitation, and you should use them whenever possible. There are still limitations to partitions. For instance, all data on the partition needs to be contiguous. Also, they cannot, by design, be resized while keeping the data. For instance, if my 40 gigabyte /home partition is almost full, I can't just resize it while shrinking the /var partition. Through brilliant programming, we found ways around some of these limitations, but those tools are tricks. For instance, if you want to resize a /home partition, a smart disc tool may resize /var and shrink the partition. Then it would move all of the data blocks in /var to the end of the disc. Lastly, it would recreate the /home partition, taking up the full available size. Keep in mind that this is not a function of BIOS or GUID partitions, but rather, it is through sheer brilliance of the software. One of the best ways of dividing up a drive in Linux is by using LVM. LVM is short for logical volume management. LVM is an abstraction layer above partitions and discs allowing us to combine those partitions or even bury discs into larger drives. We can resize them up or down, move them,
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/hard-drive-partitioning-strategies?u=76281980&t=189)** create redundant arrays or raids, spread volumes across multiple drives, and more. In short, it's a pretty fantastic thing. Some distributions don't install on LVM by default, but if they can, I'd recommend doing it. CentOS 7 installs using LVM by default. We have another disc option during install, and that is whether to encrypt the drive or not. Encryption used to be very slow, but now that we have AES [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) encryption and decryption built into CPUs, there's very little, if any, slowdown with an encrypted drive. For mobile devices such as laptops, I highly recommend encrypting the drives. This removes a lot of stress if the device gets misplaced. You're out the hardware, but nothing on it can be accessed. The last thing to discuss is swap space. We need swap space for those times when we run out of real memory. With Linux, the minimum size is the amount of memory, and the ideal size is twice the size of the memory. If we don't have enough swap space, we can add more with another partition or LVM later. In an emergency, we can even create a swap file similar to a [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) page file. We've talked about how to divide up the OS, but we haven't talked about why we do this. Windows uses one large partition for its operating system. Creating partitions seems unnecessarily complex, so let me explain. If you run out of space on your OS drive, your computer comes to a standstill because it may not be able to write to temporary files or swap data. Having that OS on a separate partition keeps other processes from filling that drive.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/hard-drive-partitioning-strategies?u=76281980&t=282)** Usually what fills a drive is variable linked files like mail spools or logs. It would make sense to put /var on its own partition. If it's full, it will affect logging but not the running of the OS, allowing us to fix it. Users may also fill drives with downloaded files. We can put /home on a separate partition to solve this. We can also use quotas to limit the amount of space they can use. By having virtual memory on a separate partition, we can use a more efficient memory mapping scheme. Instead of the OS having to access the file system which then allows us to read the swap file, the OS can access it directly which is faster. Another thing is mount options. If there's no reason to have an executable file in /var, then we can mount it without that ability. If there's no reason to write data to a partition, we can mount it read only. If there's no reason for a user to elevate privileges via an SUID or SGID application, then we can mount our partition, no SUID or no SGID. There are a lot of options with mounting that make the case for our partitioned OS stronger.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) (5), [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) (2), [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) (2)

#### [List, create, delete partitions on MBR and GPT disks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/list-create-delete-partitions-on-mbr-and-gpt-disks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/list-create-delete-partitions-on-mbr-and-gpt-disks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Male] Let's create some partitions now. Power up your VM, log in, and open a terminal. The first thing we want to do is get a list of drives in the system. We can do this by catting proc partitions. In a terminal, type in cat /proc/partitions, and hit enter. This information comes from the kernel directly. This is the list of drives and partitions that the kernel currently recognizes. Another tool that's useful in determining the available drives is lsblk. Type in lsblk, and hit enter. This also gives a visual of the drives and their partitions, or logical volumes. We can see that /dev/sda has partitions on it, and they're mounted as /boot, and /, but /dev/sdb is bare. This is one of our new drives. If you want to read the partition table for all drives directly, you can use fdisk -l. Type in clear, and then type in sudo fdisk -l, and hit enter, type in your password if prompted. This will show all partitions on all drives. Also, note that this shows what type of a partition table a drive might have. To get a list of all partitions on /dev/sdb, we will need to specify it. Type in clear, and then type in sudo fdisk -l /dev/sdb, and hit enter. To partition our bare/dev/sdb drive, we can use one of three commands. Fdisk, gdisk, or parted. Legacy systems use a BIOS to boot the machine, and the BIOS stores its partition table in the MBR,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/list-create-delete-partitions-on-mbr-and-gpt-disks?u=76281980&t=95)** or Master Boot Record. Fdisk is the legacy tool for BIOS MBR based systems. MBR systems have a limitations of four real partitions, otherwise known as primary partitions, and accessible size of two TB. If you want more partitions, one of these four needs to be an extended partition. Inside this extended partition, we can then create logical partitions. All primary, and extended partitions will be numbered one through four. All logical partitions will be numbered five and higher. There are actually three different fdisk programs, fdisk, sfdisk, and cfdisk. Fdisk is the interactive tool, sfdisk is command line only and non interactive for scripts, all arguments are provided on the command line, and carried out. Cfdisk is a curses based application that's somewhat visual. Newer systems use UEFI, and with that GUID partition table, or GPT. As of this recording, experimental GPT support has been added to fdisk. Gdisk however, was designed for GPT partitions. With GPT, you can have virtually unlimited partitions, but some programs only support 128. GPT has an accessible size of 18 exabytes. Also gdisk stores a BIOS partition table along with a GPT table, so even non-UEFI systems can use the GPT table. Like fdisk, we have three gdisk programs. Gdisk, sgisk, for scripts, and cgdisk, for a curses interface. For this video, we'll be using gdisk.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/list-create-delete-partitions-on-mbr-and-gpt-disks?u=76281980&t=189)** Another tool is parted, which has additional power, which we'll cover in another video. Older versions also formatted partitions and repaired them, some of this functionality has been removed. Let's run gdisk with /dev/sdb. Type in clear, then type in sudo gdisk /dev/sdb, and hit enter. Let's press ? For the help menu, and hit enter. We can see that we can press N to add a new partition, D to delete a partition, and P to print the partition table, lastly we can press W to write the partition table. Let's create a new partition by pressing N, and hitting enter. For the partition number, take the default and hit enter again. For the first sector, hit enter again. For the ending sector, I'm going to specify a size. Type in +500M, capital M for megabytes. This will create a 500MB partition and leave the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the disk free. Hit enter for the default partition ID of 8300. To verify, press P to print your partition table. Lastly, we'll press W to write the partition table, and hit enter. Note that until we press W, no changes have been saved to the disk. If we decide not to change the partition table, we can exit, and everything will remain the same. Press Y to commit. Now cat proc partitions to see if the kernel recognizes the partition. Type in clear, then type in cat /proc/partitions, and hit enter. Here we see the sdb1 partition we just created. We can also use gdisk -l to list the partitions
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/list-create-delete-partitions-on-mbr-and-gpt-disks?u=76281980&t=284)** in the partition table. Type in sudo gdisk -l /dev/sdb, and hit enter. Note that gdisk -l isn't system wide like fdisk -l, it only lists partitions on the specified drive. Also note, that catting proc partitions shows a partition table according to the kernel. Gdisk -l shows what has been saved on the drive, in the partition table. These two things are not the same. If for some reason the kernel doesn't update its record of the partitions, in RAM, there will be a discrepancy between what's in proc partitions, and what is on the actual disk. Generally, the partitioning tool warns you of this. In some cases, we can just run the partprobe command to force the kernel to reload the partition table. Type in clear, then type in sudo partprobe, and hit enter. If you don't get any output from partprobe, then the partition tables are in sync. Lastly, let's put a file system on our new partition. Type in sudo mkfs -t ext4 /dev/sdb1, and hit enter.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/list-create-delete-partitions-on-mbr-and-gpt-disks?u=76281980&t=352)** Now this partition is ready to be mounted.

#### [Create parititons using parted](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-parititons-using-parted?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-parititons-using-parted?u=76281980&t=0)** - Parted is a lot like fDisk gDisk because it creates partitions. Older versions can also resize, copy and move volume as well format and rescue them. Some of these features where removed after Version 2.4 including check, cp, mkpartfs, move and resize. Parted can be used in interactive and non interactive modes. Let's start by installing Parted, if it isn't already. Type in sudo space yum space install space -y space parted. Type in your password at prompted. If it is install, go ahead and run it. Type in sudo space parted and hit enter. We will start out by typing in Help. The Help command shows all sub commands. To get more help on a specific sub command such as the Print command. We can type in help space print and hit enter. We can see that there is a print all option. Let's type in print space all and hit enter. This shows all drives and partitions in a way that is very similar to fdisk -l that we saw in the previous video. To get a short concise list of drives, type in print space devices, hit enter. I like this output, as I can quickly assess which drives are in the system. It is similar to the output of lsblk. Notice that we didn't specify drive when we started Parted like we did with gdisk. This is because we can change the drives from within Parted. To select a different drives such as dev sdc, type in select /dev/sdc and hit enter.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-parititons-using-parted?u=76281980&t=95)** Now we are editing /dev/sdc, to get some stats on the drive and show free space, type in print space free and hit enter. We see that our drive doesn't even have a partition table on it. This is because we created a bare rod driven virtual box. Usually when we buy a physical hard drive, it comes with the partition table already. Let's create a partition table. We can do this with a mklabel or mktable sub commands as they do the same thing. To create a MS-DOS type partition table, type in mklabel space msdos and hit enter. We can verify this with print. Now that we have a partition table, let's create a new partition. Type in mkpart space primary space 1 space 500MB with no space. The choice of type is primary, extended or logical because we created a GPT partition table everything will be primary. We made a 500 MegaBytes because we purposely reserving part of the drive for later. Hit enter and we going to verify with print. You might want to know that there is a difference between 1 GB and 1 GiB. 1GB is a thousand MegaBytes and 1 GiB is one thousand twenty four MegaBytes. Keep this in mind. Since formatting has been removed from this version of Parted, we will now exit and use other tools to format it. Type in quit and hit enter. To put a file system on it, type in clear and then type in sudo space mkfs space -t space
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-parititons-using-parted?u=76281980&t=189)** ext4 space /dev/sdc1 and hit enter. We are done creating a partition using Parted and formatting it using mkfs.

#### [Resize partitions without losing data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/resize-partitions-without-losing-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/resize-partitions-without-losing-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Resizing partitions can be daunting, but it's really not that hard, and as long as you take good notes, not that risky. As long as we're resizing our partitions larger, we can even do this with a drive mounted for most file systems, although it is still safer to unmount them first. The process starts by taking note of the old partition boundaries. I'd recommend writing them down or taking a screenshot. Then, we edit the partition table and delete the partition. Just because we deleted the partition, does not mean we've deleted the data. Remember that the master boot record is only 512 bytes and the GPT partition table is about 1500. Partition editing software only to use a very small portion of the disk. Then, we create a new larger partition and resize the file system. If you miss any of these steps, the usable size won't increase. Before we go further, let's mount up slash dev slash SDB one so we can demonstrate resizing a live volume. First, make a temporary mount point. Type in sudo space M-K dir space slash media slash SDB one and hit enter. Then type in your password as prompted. Now, let's mount the drive. Type in sudo space mount space slash dev slash SDB one space slash media slash SDB one and hit enter. We can verify this with a LSBLK command. We can see that our slash dev slash SDB one partition is mounted, and that it's about 500 megabytes in size. Now let's edit the partition table of slash dev slash SDB using G disk. Type in sudo space G disk space slash dev
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/resize-partitions-without-losing-data?u=76281980&t=96)** slash SDB and hit enter. Once it's loaded, press P to print the partition table. Here, we can see the start sector of SDB one is 2048. The ending sector really is not that important as we're going to change it. You can either write this information down or do a screenshot and save it. To take a screenshot with the virtual box, you can press your host key plus E. In my case, my host key is right control. Give this a name and save it. Note that we are not getting a VM snapshot, but rather saving a graphic of the screen. Now, let's delete the first partition and recreate it. Press D and hit enter. If you had more than one partition, it would prompt you to specify the correct one. Since we only have one, it will delete it straight away. Now press P to verify that it's gone. Now we need to create a new partition. We need to make sure everything is the same as the old partition except for the ending sector. Press N for new and hit enter. For partition number, enter the old number. Mine was one, which is the default. Hit enter. For a starting sector, enter the old number again. Mine was 2048, which is the default again, so hit enter. For our ending sector, take the default, which will be the last sector of the disk. Our old partition type was [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) file system, so accept the default again. Let's view the partition table by pressing P and hitting Enter. We should now see a new larger one gigabyte partition. Press W to write, and then Y to confirm. If an error message comes up saying
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/resize-partitions-without-losing-data?u=76281980&t=188)** the kernel is still using the old partition table, it's because the partition is locked by being mounted. To fix this without rebooting, just unmount it and then use the part probe command. Type in clear and then type in sudo space U mount space slash dev slash SDB one and hit enter. Now run part probe to force the kernel to reread the partition table. Type in sudo space part probe and hit enter. And now remount it. Type in sudo space mount space slash dev slash SDB one space slash media slash SDB one and hit enter. Now, let's verify the partition is larger using LSBLK. LSBLK verifies it is now about one gigabyte. Let's also check the file system size. Type in D-F space dash H and hit enter. Here we see that the mounted volume is still its old size. This is because we enlarged the container around the file system, but not the file system itself. To resize the file system, we'll use resize 2FS. This command is only for EXD file systems. If we were using XFS file system, then we use the XFS underscore grow FX command. Type in sudo space resize 2FS space slash dev slash SDB one and hit enter. By default, resize 2FS uses all available space. Now let's check with DF again. Type in DF space dash H and hit enter. We can how see that SDB one is 931 megabytes,
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/resize-partitions-without-losing-data?u=76281980&t=281)** and our resize has been successful.

#### [Basic features of logical volume management (LVM)](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-features-of-logical-volume-management-lvm?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-features-of-logical-volume-management-lvm?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] LVM stands for logical volume management. LVM version one was created 1998. LVM version two, which is the version we use today, was released with the two point six kernel in 2003. LVM two comprises of 42 commands and another 18 built-in so it's not a simple system. Not every distribution uses LVM by default. With LVM a volume size can be dynamically expanded or reduced. When expanding the disk the volume does not have to be unmounted and the system can continue to run while the operation is underway. To reduce the size of the volume, it needs to be unmounted. Small tons of unused space from several disks can be combined to create a usable volume. For instance, if there is five gigabytes of free space on five different disks, it can be combined into one usable volume. LVM supports disk spanning. It can combine two disks into one drive. If data redundancy is needed LVM can store and update up to six identical copies of the data. We can also have fail over copies. If one copy fails another redundant copy can be created automatically. Volumes can be configured so data is distributed across disks, speeding up access. LVM supports striping data in various raid formats for redundancy and speed. There can be multiple redundant links to a drive. If one link goes down LVM will automatically switch to another. If a physical drive is failing, a volume can be moved to a new physical disk. Flash disks can act as a cache drive for a spinning drive. In the case where we have a small, fast,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-features-of-logical-volume-management-lvm?u=76281980&t=93)** solid state disk and a large spinning disk, we can use the fast solid state disk to act as a cache device. There are cluster extensions to allow LVM to be used in clusters for high availability. Volumes can be snap shotted to freeze the data in time. The volume can still be used during this process. This is useful for backups. The current state of the disk can be reverted back to a pervious state to undo failed upgrades using snap shots. LVM allows us to thin provision disks. This means we can create a 10 terabyte volume when we only have three terabytes of space. When the three terabyte starts to fill up, we can add more physical disks.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** lvm (12)
> **CLI Commands:** snap (2)
> **UI Navigation:** switch to (1)
> **Definitions:** stands for (1)
> **Analogies:** for instance (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)

#### [Manage LVM volumes and volume groups](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-lvm-volumes-and-volume-groups?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-lvm-volumes-and-volume-groups?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are limitations to standard partitions. For instance, they have to be continuous on the disk. If you want to resize a partition in the middle of a disk, you're out of luck. There are tools such as GParted that can manage this through sheer brilliance, but you should always back up your drives before attempting a resize. Logical volume management provides tools to avoid this situation by design. If there's room in a volume group, you can resize the logical volume even if the empty space is somewhere else on the disk. If there is no room in the volume group, you can just add more drives to it on the fly and then the logical volume can be expanded across all drives. To use a driver partition with LVM, we need to make it a physical volume, or PV, using the pvcreate command. Note that you should do this on an empty, unmounted partition or drive. Any data on the drive will be destroyed. Let's make sure that /dev/sdb1 is not mounted. In a terminal, type in sudo space umount space /dev/sdb1 and hit Enter. Type in your password if prompted. In newer versions of LVM, devices will be automatically made into physical volumes when added to volume groups. For educational purposes and troubleshooting reasons, I like to do this step manually. Type in sudo space pvcreate space /dev/sdb1 and hit Enter. It may notice that there's a file system on it already. Press Y to proceed. Now let's verify this by typing in sudo space pvs and hit Enter. Here we can see /dev/sdb1. If you're using an entire raw device, such as /dev/sdb, and that drive is physically
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-lvm-volumes-and-volume-groups?u=76281980&t=96)** on a solid state disk, you'll want to add additional arguments to align the data. Now, let's create a volume group, or VG, including a new PV. We'll use vgcreate for this. Type in clear, and then type in sudo space vgcreate space the volume group name, which I'm going to call it vgdata, space and then PVs you want to include. In our case, it's just /dev/sdb1. If we had more than one, we could put a space and add it at the end. Hit Enter. We're going to verify this with sudo space vgs, or VG summary. Now that we have a volume group, let's create a logical volume, or LV, in it. We'll use lvcreate for this. Type in clear, and then type in sudo space lvcreate space --name space lvdata space --size space 500 capital M for megabytes space vgdata, vgdata being the volume group, and hit Enter. We'll verify this by typing in sudo space lvs. Here we can see our logical volume in our volume group and the size. There are two different paths that you can use to refer to the logical volumes, /dev/VolumeGroupname/LogicalVolume name, or we can also use /dev/mapper/VolumeGroupname-LogicalVolumename. Either work fine, but I prefer the former. You can even place hyphens in the name of a volume group or a logical volume. However, LVM will insert two hyphens,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-lvm-volumes-and-volume-groups?u=76281980&t=191)** one escapes the other. If the name was vg-data, some tools will print out the path as /dev/mapper/vg--data-lvdata. It doesn't hurt anything, but it may be confusing. We'll need to format our logical volume with a file system. We have several choices for file systems. The historic [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) file system is ext2, which supports permissions and ownership but is not journalized. Ext3 is ext2 but with a journal. The newest in the series is ext4, which supports much larger drives than the ext3, about 32,000 times larger at one million terabytes. Ext4 is more robust than ext3 and supports solid state drives as well. Ext4 is a good default file system for Linux. Ext2 and three drives should be migrated to ext4. However, the new default in Enterprise Linux 7 is XFS, a file system created by Silicon Graphics for its version of UNIX. It supports much larger drives than even ext4 and larger maximum file sizes. It supports snapshots as well. Even though it's easy to resize an XFS file system larger, it's very, very difficult to resize it smaller. The last one is Btrfs, or butter file system. Butter file system is the most capable but currently should be considered a technology preview. It's not as fast as the others, nor is it as mature. It, however, has some amazing features that largely replace the Linux RAID subsystem, LVM, and file systems all in one. Now to get your LVM path to show up, we may, on occasion, have to activate it using vgchange.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-lvm-volumes-and-volume-groups?u=76281980&t=286)** Type in sudo space vgchange space -ay and hit Enter. You don't always have to do this, but it doesn't hurt anything to do it. Let's format our logical volume as ext4. Type in clear and then type in sudo space mkfs space -t space ext4 space /dev and the path would be vgdata/lvdata. So the whole path would be /dev/vgdata/lvdata, and hit Enter. We can verify the file system as blkid. Type in sudo space blkid and hit Enter. And there we have it at the end. We have our logical volume, and at the end, it says TYPE="ext4". Now it's mounted on a new mount point. First let's create the mount point using mkdir. Type in clear, type in sudo space mkdir space /media/lvdata and hit Enter. And then we'll mount it with sudo space mount space /dev/vgdata/lvdata space /media/lvdata, and hit Enter. And we can verify this with df. Type in df space -h, and we'll see the size of it and that it's mounted.

#### [Replace a physical volume](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/replace-a-physical-volume?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/replace-a-physical-volume?u=76281980&t=0)** - Replacing a physical drive is a necessary task in the real world. Drives often need to be replaced due to mechanical failure. You may also want to migrate to larger drives as they become available. Let's start by reviewing our drives. Type in 'sudo lsblk', and hit enter. Type in your password, if prompted. We can see that our sdb and sdc drives are 1 Gigabyte each. Our sdb1 partition is 1 Gigabyte, and our sdc1 partition is about 500 Megabytes. Looking at our pvs with 'pvs' verifies this. Type in 'sudo pvs' and hit enter. >
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/replace-a-physical-volume?u=76281980&t=62)** and hit enter. And we'll verify this with lvs. Type in 'sudo lvs' and hit enter. Now, our logical volume is about 1.5 Gigabytes, or the entire space of the vg. It's comprised of a 1 Gigabyte pv, and a 500 Megabyte pv. Because the logical volume is larger than any of our pvs, this means it spans them both. This is what we want for this exercise. Now let's add a third pv to our volume group, and then we'll move the data from our 2nd pv to the 3rd one, and remove the 2nd to simulate replacing a real, physical hard drive. Type in 'clear', and then type in 'sudo gdisk /dev/sdd', and hit enter. Press n for new partition, take the default for partition number, take the default for sector number, for size we're going to say '+500M', and hit enter. Take the default for partition type, and press 'w' to write, and then 'y' to confirm. Now let's verify this with 'sudo lsblk', and hit enter. Now we have an extra 500 Megabyte partition we can use. Let's add it to our vgdata volume group. This time, we're going to skip the pv create step and let lvm do it for us. >
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/replace-a-physical-volume?u=76281980&t=152)** And let's verify this with vgs. Type in 'sudo vgs' and hit enter. > **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/replace-a-physical-volume?u=76281980&t=222)** and hit enter. And we'll verify again with pvs. Type in 'sudo pvs', and hit enter. We can now see that our /dev/sdc1 drive no longer belongs to the volume group. Now we can physically remove it from the system.

#### [Create EXT filesystems](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-ext-filesystems?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-101-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-ext-filesystems?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The standard tool to format file systems is MKFS.
